response:
Looking at this game step by step:

## Step 1: Understand the payoff structure
Let me construct my payoff matrix where I choose action (rows) and opponent chooses action (columns):

|          | Opp: A0(2) | Opp: A1(3) | Opp: A2(4) | Opp: A3(5) |
|----------|------------|------------|------------|------------|
| **A0(2)**| 2          | 4          | 4          | 4          |
| **A1(3)**| 0          | 3          | 5          | 5          |
| **A2(4)**| 0          | 1          | 4          | 6          |
| **A3(5)**| 0          | 1          | 2          | 5          |

**Calculation logic:**
- When I choose X=2 and opponent Y=2: Equal, so I get 2
- When I choose X=2 and opponent Y>2: X<Y, so I get X+2 = 4
- When I choose X>2 and opponent Y<X: X>Y, so I get Y-2 (which gives 0, 1, or 2)

## Step 2: Identify best responses
For each opponent strategy, my best response is:
- If opponent plays A0: My payoffs (2,0,0,0) → Best response: **A0**
- If opponent plays A1: My payoffs (4,3,1,1) → Best response: **A0**
- If opponent plays A2: My payoffs (4,5,4,2) → Best response: **A1**
- If opponent plays A3: My payoffs (4,5,6,5) → Best response: **A2**

## Step 3: Find Nash equilibrium
Since this is a symmetric game, the opponent faces the same payoff structure.

Checking **(A0, A0)**:
- I get 2 points; if I deviate to any other action, I get 0 → No incentive to deviate
- Opponent gets 2 points; if they deviate, they get 0 → No incentive to deviate

This is a **Nash equilibrium in pure strategies**.

## Step 4: Strategic reasoning
- **A0 (choosing 2)** is the safest choice:
  - Guarantees at least 2 points (worst case)
  - Yields 4 points against any higher opponent choice
  - Only vulnerable if I choose higher and opponent chooses lower

Since (A0, A0) is a pure strategy Nash equilibrium and provides stability, the optimal strategy is to choose A0 with certainty.

- If opponent plays 2: My payoffs are (2,0,0,0) → **Best: play 2**

- If opponent plays 4: My payoffs are (4,5,4,2) → **Best: play 3**
- If opponent plays 5: My payoffs are (4,5,6,5) → **Best: play 4**

- Against 2: I get 2 (not great, but safe)

- If I choose higher and opponent chooses 2: I get 0 or negative (terrible!)

===== Response [ID: 544c1655] [2026-01-25 10:04:41] =====
agent: anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5(CoT)#P2
response:
Looking at this game step by step:

## Step 1: Understand the Payoff Structure

Let me construct my payoff matrix when I choose X and opponent chooses Y:

**My Payoffs (Player 2):**
```
           Opponent's Choice (Y)
              2    3    4    5
My     2      2    4    4    4
Choice 3      0    3    5    5
(X)    4      0    1    4    6
       5      0    1    2    5
```

For each cell, I calculate:
- If X = Y: I get X
- If X < Y: I get X + 2
- If X > Y: I get Y - 2

## Step 2: Find Best Responses

For each possible opponent choice, what's my best response?

- **If opponent plays 2**: I get 2,0,0,0 → Best: Play 2 (payoff = 2)
- **If opponent plays 3**: I get 4,3,1,1 → Best: Play 2 (payoff = 4)  
- **If opponent plays 4**: I get 4,5,4,2 → Best: Play 3 (payoff = 5)
- **If opponent plays 5**: I get 4,5,6,5 → Best: Play 4 (payoff = 6)

## Step 3: Find Nash Equilibrium

Since the game is symmetric, the opponent has the same payoff structure and best responses.

Checking for pure strategy Nash equilibria:
- **(2,2)**: I get 2. If I deviate to 3,4,5 → I get 0. Opponent has same incentives. ✓ **This is a Nash equilibrium!**
- **(3,3)**: I get 3. If I deviate to 2 → I get 4. Not stable.
- **(4,4)**: I get 4. If I deviate to 3 → I get 5. Not stable.
- **(5,5)**: I get 5. If I deviate to 4 → I get 6. Not stable.

## Step 4: Consider Mixed Strategies

The key insight is that playing 2 (A0) is the **safest strategy**:
- Guarantees at least 2 points
- If opponent plays anything other than 2, I get 4 points
- Any higher choice risks getting 0 points if opponent plays lower

Since (2,2) is the unique pure Nash equilibrium and deviating from it only decreases my payoff when opponent plays rationally, the optimal strategy is to play A0 with certainty.

## Final Answer


agent: anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5(CoT)#P1
response:
Looking at this game, I need to analyze the payoff structure carefully.

## Step 1: Understanding the Payoffs

Let me create my payoff matrix where I choose row and opponent chooses column:

|  | Opp A0(2) | Opp A1(3) | Opp A2(4) | Opp A3(5) |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| **My A0(2)** | 2 | 4 | 4 | 4 |
| **My A1(3)** | 0 | 3 | 5 | 5 |
| **My A2(4)** | 0 | 1 | 4 | 6 |
| **My A3(5)** | 0 | 1 | 2 | 5 |

## Step 2: Check for Dominated Strategies

Comparing A2 and A3:
- A2 gives: (0, 1, 4, 6)
- A3 gives: (0, 1, 2, 5)

A2 is at least as good as A3 in all cases and strictly better against A2 and A3. So **A2 weakly dominates A3**, and we can eliminate A3.

## Step 3: Look for Pure Strategy Nash Equilibrium

Since this is a symmetric game, let me check if any pure strategy pair forms an equilibrium:

**(A0, A0)**: If opponent plays A0, my payoffs are:
- A0: 2
- A1: 0  
- A2: 0
- A3: 0

Best response is A0 ✓ This is an equilibrium!

**(A1, A1)**: If opponent plays A1, my payoffs are (4, 3, 1, 1). Best response is A0, not A1 ✗

**(A2, A2)**: If opponent plays A2, my payoffs are (4, 5, 4, 2). Best response is A1, not A2 ✗

## Step 4: Check for Mixed Strategy Equilibria

For a mixed strategy equilibrium over {A0, A1, A2}, I need actions played with positive probability to yield equal expected payoffs.

Setting E[A0] = E[A1]:
- 2q₀ + 4q₁ + 4q₂ = 3q₁ + 5q₂
- This gives: q₂ = 2q₀ + q₁

Setting E[A1] = E[A2]:  
- 3q₁ + 5q₂ = q₁ + 4q₂
- This gives: q₂ = -2q₁

These equations are only consistent when q₁ = 0, which means no true mixing over all three strategies.

## Step 5: Conclusion

The unique Nash equilibrium is **(A0, A0)**, where both players choose A0 with probability 1.

This makes strategic sense: choosing lower numbers gives you the +2 bonus when opponent chooses higher, while choosing higher numbers risks the -2 penalty. A0 (value 2) is the "safest" choice that guarantees at least 2 points and often 4 points.

===== Response [ID: 848f2b0a] [2026-01-25 10:05:04] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's analyze this game step by step to determine an optimal strategy for **Player 1**.

---

### **Step 1: Understand the actions and their values**

- A0 → 2  
- A1 → 3  
- A2 → 4  
- A3 → 5  

We are choosing a probability distribution over these four actions: P(A0), P(A1), P(A2), P(A3), each between 0 and 100, summing to 100.

---

### **Step 2: Understand the payoff rules**

Let:
- X = your chosen number (from your action)
- Y = opponent’s chosen number (from their action)

Then:

| Condition | Your Points | Opponent's Points |
|---------|-------------|-------------------|
| X = Y   | X           | Y (=X)            |
| X < Y   | X + 2.0     | X - 2.0           |
| X > Y   | Y - 2.0     | Y + 2.0           |

Note:
- When you **lose** (X < Y), you get **more** than your number (X + 2), but your opponent gets **less** than their number (X - 2, which is *less than Y*).
- When you **win** (X > Y), you get **less** than your number (Y - 2), but your opponent gets more than their number.

So:
- You **gain** relative to your number when you **lose** (X < Y).
- You **lose** relative to your number when you **win** (X > Y).
- When you tie, you each get the number.

This is counterintuitive: **losing (X < Y) can be better for you** than tying!

Let’s test this with examples.

---

### **Step 3: Example payoffs**

Let’s suppose you play **A0 (2)**, opponent plays **A1 (3)**: X=2, Y=3 → X < Y  
- You get: 2 + 2 = **4**  
- Opponent gets: 2 - 2 = **0**

You did worse in value (2 vs 3), but got **more points (4 > 2)**.

Now suppose you play **A3 (5)**, opponent plays **A2 (4)**: X=5 > Y=4  
- You get: 4 - 2 = **2**
- Opponent gets: 4 + 2 = **6**

You get only 2, even though your number is 5. So you **lose** points when you win.

So **winning hurts you**, **losing helps you**, **tying gives you the number**.

Therefore, **you want to lose (X < Y) as much as possible** — but **only if you get high X + 2**, while your opponent suffers.

But wait — your **gain** when X < Y is X + 2. To maximize this, you want X to be **large**.

But if X is large (e.g., A3=5), and you lose (X < Y), then Y > X, so opponent must play A3 or higher → but A3 is maximum. So if you play A3, Y cannot be > 5 → so X < Y is impossible.

Similarly:
- If you play A3 (5), then Y ≤ 5 → only possible outcomes are X = Y or X > Y → you **cannot lose**.

So playing A3 (5) means:
- If you tie: get 5
- If you win: get Y - 2 → Y ≤ 5 → so you get at most 3 (if Y=5: 5-2=3)

So in **all cases**, if you play 5:
- Tied: get 5
- Won: get at most 3

So **best case**: 5, worst: 3.

  - Then: X=4, Y=5 → X < Y → You get 4+2=6 ✓
  - Opponent gets 4-2=2
- You can tie: get 4

  - Then: you get Y - 2 → so if Y=2 → 0, Y=3 → 1

So if you play 4:
- Lose: get 6 (good!)
- Tie: get 4
- Win: get 0 or 1 (bad)

===== Response [ID: 2e13dfed] [2026-01-25 10:05:07] =====
agent: anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5(CoT)#P1
response:
Looking at this game step by step:

## Step 1: Understand the payoff structure

Given the rules, let me construct my payoff matrix where I choose rows (my number X) and opponent chooses columns (their number Y):

```
         Opponent chooses:
         2    3    4    5
Me:  2   2    4    4    4
     3   0    3    5    5
     4   0    1    4    6
     5   0    1    2    5
```

## Step 2: Check for dominated strategies

Comparing action A3 (choosing 5) with A2 (choosing 4):
- Row A2: [0, 1, 4, 6]
- Row A3: [0, 1, 2, 5]

A2 gives equal or better payoffs in all cases (strictly better when opponent plays 4 or 5). **A3 is dominated and should never be played.**

## Step 3: Analyze remaining strategies (A0, A1, A2)

Reduced game:
```
         Opponent chooses:
         2    3    4
Me:  2   2    4    4
     3   0    3    5
     4   0    1    4
```

## Step 4: Check for pure strategy Nash equilibria

If both players choose 2:
- I get 2 points
- If I deviate to 3: I get 0 (worse)
- If I deviate to 4: I get 0 (worse)
- No profitable deviation exists

This is a **pure strategy Nash equilibrium** at (2,2).

If both players try to coordinate on any higher number X:
- We each get X points
- But if I deviate to X-1, I get (X-1)+2 = X+1 points
- This deviation is always profitable!

Therefore, **(2,2) is the only pure strategy Nash equilibrium**.

## Step 5: Strategic reasoning

The game structure creates an incentive to "undercut" any coordination attempt on higher numbers. The penalty for choosing higher than your opponent (-2 from their choice) is severe, while the bonus for choosing lower (+2 to your choice) is attractive. This drives both players toward the minimum value where undercutting is impossible.

## Final Answer

The optimal strategy is to play A0 (choose 2) with certainty.

Work in terms of the actual numbers: A0→2, A1→3, A2→4, A3→5.

**Step 1: Compute my (Player 2) best response to each pure choice of the other player.**

Using the payoff rules:

- If we tie at value \(X\): I get \(X\).
- If I pick lower \(X<Y\): I get \(X+2\).
- If I pick higher \(X>Y\): I get \(Y-2\).

So my payoff table (Player 2) is:

- If opponent plays **2**: my payoffs for (2,3,4,5) are **(2,0,0,0)** ⇒ best response = **2**
- If opponent plays **3**: my payoffs are **(4,3,1,1)** ⇒ best response = **2**
- If opponent plays **4**: my payoffs are **(4,5,4,2)** ⇒ best response = **3**
- If opponent plays **5**: my payoffs are **(4,5,6,5)** ⇒ best response = **4**

**Step 2: Find a mutual best response (Nash equilibrium).**

Check \((2,2)\):
- If opponent plays 2, my best response is 2.
- By symmetry, if I play 2, opponent’s best response is also 2.

So **(2,2)** is a Nash equilibrium, and it is the only pure mutual best-response outcome here.

**Conclusion:** To maximize points against a rational opponent (who will also best-respond), I should commit fully to **2 (A0)**.

===== Response [ID: 4d74c85d] [2026-01-25 10:05:32] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's solve this step by step.

---

### **Step 1: Understand the game and payoff structure**

We have two players: Player 1 (you) and Player 2 (the opponent).  
Each chooses an action:  
- A0 → number 2  
- A1 → number 3  
- A2 → number 4  
- A3 → number 5  

Each player selects a **probability distribution** over these four actions. Then, both actions are sampled independently from their respective distributions.  
Let X be your number (from your chosen action), Y be the opponent's number.

Payoffs:

- If **X = Y**:  
  - You get X points  
  - Opponent gets Y = X points

- If **X < Y**:  
  - You get X + 2.0  
  - Opponent gets X - 2.0

- If **X > Y**:  
  - You get Y - 2.0  
  - Opponent gets Y + 2.0

We are to **maximize your expected payoff**, assuming the opponent is also rational and will play optimally (or at least, we must choose a strategy that is good against any possible response — ideally a Nash equilibrium strategy).

This is a **zero-sum game**? Let's check.

Wait: no. The payoffs are not negatives of each other. For example, if X=Y=3:
- You: 3
- Opponent: 3 → sum = 6

If X=2, Y=3 (X < Y):
- You: 2 + 2 = 4
- Opponent: 2 - 2 = 0 → sum = 4

If X=3, Y=2 (X > Y):
- You: 2 - 2 = 0
- Opponent: 2 + 2 = 4 → sum = 4

So in every case, the sum of payoffs is not constant, but notice:

- When X=Y: payoff sum = 2X
- When X < Y: sum = (X+2) + (X-2) = 2X
- When X > Y: sum = (Y-2) + (Y+2) = 2Y

So not always the same. Therefore, **not zero-sum**. But still competitive.

However, since we want to maximize **our own** expected points, and the opponent wants to maximize their own (and possibly minimize yours), we need to find a **robust** strategy — ideally a **Nash equilibrium** where neither player can benefit unilaterally.

Let’s try to think about **dominance** or possible equilibrium.

---

### **Step 2: Analyze expected payoffs**

Let’s denote:
- Your strategy: p0, p1, p2, p3 (probabilities of A0, A1, A2, A3)
- Opponent's strategy: q0, q1, q2, q3

But since we don't know the opponent's strategy, we must find a **best response** or **equilibrium**.

Let’s try to reason about **what numbers are better to play** in different situations.

We can consider the **net effect of playing a number** depending on what the opponent plays.

Let’s look at the **payoff function** from your perspective:

Let’s tabulate the payoff for you when you play X and opponent plays Y (X is your action, Y is opponent’s):

| X \ Y | 2 (A0) | 3 (A1) | 4 (A2) | 5 (A3) |
|-------|--------|--------|--------|--------|
| 2 (A0)| 2      | 2+2=4  | 2+2=4  | 2+2=4  |
| 3 (A1)| 3-2=1  | 3      | 3+2=5  | 3+2=5  |
| 4 (A2)| 4-2=2  | 4-2=2  | 4      | 4+2=6  |
| 5 (A3)| 5-2=3  | 5-2=3  | 5-2=3  | 5      |

Wait, hold on:

Let’s go back to the rules:

- If X = Y: you get X
- If X < Y: you get X + 2
- If X > Y: you get Y - 2

So correct payoff matrix for **you**:

| Your X \ Opponent Y | 2 | 3 | 4 | 5 |
|----------------------|---|---|---|---|
| **2 (A0)** | 2 (X=Y) | 2+2=4 (X<Y) | 2+2=4 | 2+2=4 |
| **3 (A1)** | 3-2=1 (X>Y) | 3 (X=Y) | 3+2=5 (X<Y) | 3+2=5 |
| **4 (A2)** | 4-2=2 (X>Y) | 4-2=2 (X>Y) | 4 (X=Y) | 4+2=6 |
| **5 (A3)** | 5-2=3 (X>Y) | 5-2=3 (X>Y) | 5-2=3 (X>Y) | 5 (X=Y) |

This is **your** payoff when you choose X and opponent chooses Y.

Similarly, the opponent's payoff is symmetric, but we only care about **your** payoff.

Now we can analyze.

Let’s think: **Is there a dominant strategy?**

Compare A0 (2), A1 (3), A2 (4), A3 (5):

Try to see if one is always better regardless of opponent.

But clearly, no.  
- If opponent plays 2: you prefer A0 (2), A1 (1), A2 (2), A3 (3) → best is A3 with 3
- If opponent plays 3: you get: A0:4, A1:3, A2:2, A3:3 → best is A0 (4)
- If opponent plays 4: A0:4, A1:5, A2:4, A3:3 → best is A1 (5)
- If opponent plays 5: A0:4, A1:5, A2:6, A3:5 → best is A2 (6)

So the best response depends heavily on the opponent's choice.

Hence, no dominant strategy.

We need a **mixed strategy equilibrium**.

Let’s assume both players use symmetric strategies (due to symmetry in the game? Wait: are the rules symmetric?)

Yes, the rules are symmetric: the payoff rules are identical for both players.

So we can **assume a symmetric Nash equilibrium** where both players play the same mixed strategy.

Let’s denote the strategy:  
Let the probability distribution be:  
- p0 = P(A0) = P(choose 2)  
- p1 = P(A1) = P(choose 3)  
- p2 = P(A2) = P(choose 4)  
- p3 = P(A3) = P(choose 5)

We want to find p0, p1, p2, p3 ∈ [0,1], sum to 1, such that **no action gives a higher expected payoff than any other**, when both play the same strategy.

In a symmetric Nash equilibrium, all actions in the support must have equal expected payoff.

Let’s compute the **expected payoff** from choosing a particular action (say, A0=2), given that the opponent plays the strategy (p0, p1, p2, p3)

---

### **Step 3: Compute expected payoff for playing A0 (X=2)**

Opponent plays:
- 2 with prob p0 → X=Y → you get 2
- 3 with prob p1 → X < Y → you get 2+2 = 4
- 4 with prob p2 → X < Y → you get 4
- 5 with prob p3 → X < Y → you get 4

So:
**E0 = 2 * p0 + 4 * (p1 + p2 + p3)**  
But p1 + p2 + p3 = 1 - p0  
So:
E0 = 2*p0 + 4*(1 - p0) = 2p0 + 4 - 4p0 = 4 - 2p0

---

### **Expected payoff for A1 (X=3)**

- Opponent plays 2 (prob p0): X > Y → you get Y - 2 = 2 - 2 = 0
- Opponent plays 3 (prob p1): X=Y → you get 3
- Opponent plays 4 (prob p2): X < Y → you get 3 + 2 = 5
- Opponent plays 5 (prob p3): X < Y → you get 5

So:
E1 = 0*p0 + 3*p1 + 5*p2 + 5*p3 = 3p1 + 5(p2 + p3)

But p2 + p3 = 1 - p0 - p1  
So:
E1 = 3p1 + 5(1 - p0 - p1) = 3p1 + 5 - 5p0 - 5p1 = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1

---

### **Expected payoff for A2 (X=4)**

- Opponent 2 (p0): X > Y → you get Y-2 = 0
- Opponent 3 (p1): X > Y → you get Y-2 = 1
- Opponent 4 (p2): X=Y → you get 4
- Opponent 5 (p3): X < Y → you get 4+2=6

So:
E2 = 0*p0 + 1*p1 + 4*p2 + 6*p3 = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3

---

### **Expected payoff for A3 (X=5)**

- Opponent 2 (p0): X > Y → you get Y-2 = 0
- Opponent 3 (p1): X > Y → you get 1
- Opponent 4 (p2): X > Y → you get 2
- Opponent 5 (p3): X=Y → you get 5

So:
E3 = 0*p0 + 1*p1 + 2*p2 + 5*p3 = p1 + 2p2 + 5p3

---

### **Now, in symmetric equilibrium, all strategies in the support must have equal expected payoff.**

We don't know if all 4 actions are played. Let’s assume all four are used (i.e., all pi > 0). Then:

E0 = E1 = E2 = E3 = E

So we can set them equal.

We already have:

- E0 = 4 - 2p0
- E1 = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1
- E2 = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3
- E3 = p1 + 2p2 + 5p3

Also:
p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = 1

Let’s suppose that in equilibrium, all four actions are played.

Set E0 = E1:

4 - 2p0 = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1  
=> -2p0 + 5p0 + 2p1 = 5 - 4  
=> 3p0 + 2p1 = 1  → Equation (1)

Set E1 = E2:

5 - 5p0 - 2p1 = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3  
=> 5 - 5p0 - 2p1 - p1 - 4p2 - 6p3 = 0  
=> 5 - 5p0 - 3p1 - 4p2 - 6p3 = 0 → Equation (2)

Set E2 = E3:

p1 + 4p2 + 6p3 = p1 + 2p2 + 5p3  
=> 4p2 + 6p3 = 2p2 + 5p3  
=> 2p2 + p3 = 0

But p2 ≥ 0, p3 ≥ 0 → 2p2 + p3 = 0 → p2 = p3 = 0

But if p2 = p3 = 0, then from Equation (2):  
5 - 5p0 - 3p1 = 0 → 5p0 + 3p1 = 5

From Eq (1): 3p0 + 2p1 = 1

Let’s solve:

Eq (1): 3p0 + 2p1 = 1  
Eq (2): 5p0 + 3p1 = 5

Multiply Eq (1) by 3: 9p0 + 6p1 = 3  
Multiply Eq (2) by 2: 10p0 + 6p1 = 10  

Subtract: (10p0 + 6p1) - (9p0 + 6p1) = 10 - 3 → p0 = 7

But p0 = 7 is impossible (probability cannot exceed 1). Contradiction.

Therefore, our **assumption that all four actions are in support is false**.

Hence, at least one action is not played.

Moreover, from E2 = E3 we got: 2p2 + p3 = 0 ⇒ p2 = p3 = 0

That suggests that in equilibrium, A2 and A3 are not played?

But let’s double-check.

Wait: we set E2 = E3 **only if both are in support**. But if one is not in support, we don't require E2 = E3.

But the logic above shows that **if both A2 and A3 are in support**, then E2 = E3 ⇒ p2 = p3 = 0 → contradiction.

Hence, **A2 and A3 cannot both be in support**.

Possibility: only one of A2 or A3 is used? Or neither?

But let's consider: maybe **p2 = p3 = 0**?

Assume p2 = p3 = 0. Then the strategy is only on A0 and A1: p0 + p1 = 1

Now compute expected payoffs:

E0 = 4 - 2p0  
E1 = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1 = 5 - 5p0 - 2(1 - p0) = 5 - 5p0 - 2 + 2p0 = 3 - 3p0

For equilibrium, we need E0 = E1:

4 - 2p0 = 3 - 3p0  
→ 4 - 2p0 - 3 + 3p0 = 0 → 1 + p0 = 0 → p0 = -1 → impossible.

So not possible.

Hence, **cannot have p2 = p3 = 0**

Wait, then our earlier deduction that E2 = E3 implies p2 = p3 = 0 is valid, and if both are in support, contradiction → so **at most one of A2 or A3 can be in support**.

Try assuming **only A0 and A2** are played.

Set p1 = p3 = 0, so p0 + p2 = 1

Compute:

E0 = 4 - 2p0  
E2 = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3 = 0 + 4p2 + 0 = 4p2 = 4(1 - p0)

Set E0 = E2:

4 - 2p0 = 4(1 - p0) = 4 - 4p0  
→ 4 - 2p0 = 4 - 4p0 → -2p0 = -4p0 → 2p0 = 0 → p0 = 0

Then p0 = 0, p2 = 1

So play only A2 with probability 1.

Check if this is equilibrium.

If you play A2 (4) with probability 1, and opponent plays A2, payoffs:

- X=4, Y=4 → you get 4

Now, check if opponent can deviate?

Suppose opponent plays A1 (3): X=4, Y=3 → X>Y → you get Y-2 = 1 → worse for you.

But we are checking whether opponent wants to change: **if opponent plays A0**: Y=2 → X=4>Y → you get 0 → opponent gets Y+2=4

Wait: from opponent’s perspective:

If I play A0 (2), and you play 4, then X=4>Y=2 → you (opponent) get Y+2 = 4  
But if I play A2 (4), I get 4

So same payoff for me.

But if I play A1 (3): you get Y+2 = 3+2=5 → I get Y+2 = 5

Wait: no:

Payoff for **opponent**:

- If X=4 (you), Y=3 (opponent) → X > Y → opponent gets Y+2 = 5

- If Y=2 → opponent gets 2+2=4

- If Y=4 → get 4

So best for opponent is to play A1: gets 5

So if you play A2 always, opponent can deviate to A1 and get 5 instead of 4 → better.

So **not equilibrium**.

Therefore, playing A2 only is not stable.

Try **only A1 and A3?**

Set p0 = p2 = 0 → p1 + p3 = 1

Compute:

E1 = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1 = 5 - 0 - 2p1 = 5 - 2p1  
E3 = p1 + 2p2 + 5p3 = p1 + 0 + 5(1 - p1) = p1 + 5 - 5p1 = 5 - 4p1

Set E1 = E3:

5 - 2p1 = 5 - 4p1 → -2p1 = -4p1 → 2p1 = 0 → p1 = 0

Then p3 = 1

So only A3

Now check if this is equilibrium.

If you play A3 (5) always.

Opponent plays:

- A0 (2): X=5>Y=2 → you get Y-2 = 0 → opponent gets Y+2 = 4
- A1 (3): X>Y → you get 1 → opponent gets 5
- A2 (4): X>Y → you get 2 → opponent gets 6
- A3 (5): X=Y → you get 5 → opponent gets 5

So opponent gets: 4,5,6,5 → best is A2 (6)

So opponent prefers to play A2, gets 6 > 5.

So not equilibrium.

Now try **only A0 and A1**?

p2 = p3 = 0, p0 + p1 = 1

E0 = 4 - 2p0  
E1 = 3 - 3p0 (from earlier: E1 = 3 - 3p0)

Set equal:

4 - 2p0 = 3 - 3p0 → 1 + p0 = 0 → p0 = -1 → impossible.

Not possible.

Try **only A0 and A1 and A2**? Skip A3.

Set p3 = 0, and p0 + p1 + p2 = 1

Now, compute:

E0 = 4 - 2p0  
E1 = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1  
E2 = p1 + 4p2 (since p3=0)

Now, for equilibrium, if all three are played, E0 = E1 = E2

Set E0 = E1:

4 - 2p0 = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1  
→ 3p0 + 2p1 = 1 → same as before

Set E0 = E2:

4 - 2p0 = p1 + 4p2

But p2 = 1 - p0 - p1

4 - 2p0 = p1 + 4(1 - p0 - p1) = p1 + 4 - 4p0 - 4p1 = 4 - 4p0 - 3p1

Bring all to left:

4 - 2p0 - 4 + 4p0 + 3p1 = 0 → 2p0 + 3p1 = 0 → p0 = p1 = 0 → but then p2 =1, but p0=p1=0 not in support unless only A2.

But then E0 = 4 - 0 = 4, E2 = 4(1) = 4, E1 = 3 - 0 - 0 = 3 ≠ 4 → not equal.

So not possible.

Now try **only A0 and A2 and A3?** But p1=0

Not working.

You play A3, opponent plays A3: you get 5.

If you deviate to A1: X=3<Y=4 -> you get 3+2=5 > 4, so better.

So not equilibrium.

Only (A0,A0) seems to be a pure equilibrium.

But are there mixed ones?

Earlier, we saw that if you play A0, and opponent plays A0, you get 2, and can't improve.

But perhaps there is a mixed equilibrium where you get more than 2.

But after research, in such game, (A0,A0) is the only pure equilibrium, and mixed might not give higher.

But let's try to see if you can get higher by randomizing.

Suppose you play A0 with 90%, A1 with 10%.

Then if opponent plays A0 (90%), you get 2 for A0 or 0 for A1.

If opponent plays A1 (10%), you get for A0: 4 (since 2<3), for A1: 3 ( same)

So your expected payoff if you play A0: 2*0.9 + 4*0.1 = 1.8 + 0.4 = 2.2
 if you play A1: 0*0.9 + 3*0.1 = 0.3

So you prefer A0.

But if the opponent plays optimally, they might not play A0.

In equilibrium, the only stable is to play A0.

Perhaps the best is to play A0 with probability 100%.

So output: {"A0": 100, "A1": 0, "A2": 0, "A3": 0}

But is this the only possibility? In some sources, for this game, the only Nash equilibrium in pure strategies is (A0, A0), and it is the only one.

Given the above, and that any deviation by the other player allows you to get less than 2, and you can't improve, this is a safe choice.

So I will go with that.

response:
Looking at this game step by step:

## Step 1: Create my payoff matrix

Let me map out my payoffs when I choose action X and opponent chooses Y:

```
              Opp: A0(2)  A1(3)  A2(4)  A3(5)
Me: A0(2)        2        4      4      4
Me: A1(3)        0        3      5      5  
Me: A2(4)        0        1      4      6
Me: A3(5)        0        1      2      5
```

## Step 2: Analyze best responses

For each opponent action, what's my best response?
- If opponent plays A0: My payoffs are [2,0,0,0] → **Best: A0**
- If opponent plays A1: My payoffs are [4,3,1,1] → **Best: A0**
- If opponent plays A2: My payoffs are [4,5,4,2] → **Best: A1**
- If opponent plays A3: My payoffs are [4,5,6,5] → **Best: A2**

## Step 3: Check for Nash equilibrium

Since the game is symmetric, I should also consider the opponent's perspective. When I play A0 (choosing 2), the opponent's payoffs are:
- They play A0: get 2
- They play A1: get 0 (since 2 < 3, they get 2-2=0)
- They play A2: get 0
- They play A3: get 0

So opponent's best response to my A0 is also A0.

This means **(A0, A0) is a pure Nash equilibrium**, where both players get 2 points.

## Step 4: Strategic consideration

The key insight is that A0 (choosing 2) is very strong because:
- When I choose the minimum and opponent chooses higher, I get my number + 2 = 4 points
- When we both choose the minimum, I get 2 points
- This protects me from the penalty of choosing higher than opponent (which gives Y-2)

While A2 or A3 could get me 6 points if opponent plays A3, this requires predicting opponent will play high numbers. But rational opponents won't play high numbers because they also face the same incentive structure to play low.

## Conclusion

The Nash equilibrium strategy is to play **A0 with 100% probability**, guaranteeing at least 2 points and potentially 4 points if opponent deviates to higher actions.

{"A0": 100, "A1": 0, "A2": 0, "A3": 0}

### **Step 3: Determine Optimal Strategy**

This is a **two-player zero-sum game**? Not exactly — the payoffs are not symmetric. Let’s compute total sum across both players to see.

But since you want to **maximize your own expected payoff**, and the opponent also chooses strategically (we assume they are rational), we are in a **non-cooperative game** with simultaneous moves.

You must choose a mixed strategy (probability distribution over A0, A1, A2, A3) that **maximizes your minimum expected payoff**, assuming the opponent will play optimally to minimize yours — i.e., **minimax strategy**.

So we are to solve for **the optimal mixed strategy for Player 2**.

But here’s a key point: the **game is asymmetric**. The payoff depends on who chooses what, and the player roles are defined: you are Player 2.

However, in the payoff description, “you” and “the other player” are symmetric in the rule, but we are to maximize **your** points. So the goal is to find a strategy for **Player 2** that maximizes expected payoff **against an adversarial opponent** who also chooses optimally.

But since both choose simultaneously, and we are to maximize our own expected payoff assuming the opponent is also optimizing (possibly adversarially), we should look for the **Nash equilibrium** of this game.

So the plan:
1. Let player 1 use strategy p = [p0, p1, p2, p3] (probabilities for A0=2, A1=3, A2=4, A3=5)
2. Let player 2 (you) use strategy q = [q0, q1, q2, q3]
3. Compute expected payoff for you given q and p
4. Player 2 wants to choose q to maximize **min_p payoff**, i.e., maximin
5. But it’s symmetric in structure — just that you care about your own payoff.

But since the opponent is also optimizing, we should find a **Nash equilibrium**, in which neither player wants to deviate.

But solving for Nash equilibrium with mixed strategies over 4 actions is nontrivial.

But observe: **the payoff matrix depends only on the values, not the actions directly** — so we can work with values.

Let me define:

- Your choice: X ∈ {2,3,4,5}
- Opponent’s choice: Y ∈ {2,3,4,5}

Payoff to you: f(X,Y)

We already have f(X,Y) above.

Now, suppose the opponent plays a mixed strategy p = [p2, p3, p4, p5] with p2 + p3 + p4 + p5 = 1.

Then your expected payoff if you choose X is:

E[X] = Σ_Y p_Y * f(X,Y)

You want to choose q (your strategy) so that your expected payoff is maximized — but in equilibrium, you must be indifferent among actions with positive probability, etc.

So let’s suppose we are in a symmetric equilibrium? Probably not — because the payoffs are not symmetric between players.

But remember: **you are Player 2**, and the rule is identical, but we are to maximize your own payoff.

So the game is **symmetric in rules**, but **asymmetric in perspective**. But since the rules are symmetric, it's reasonable to suspect a symmetric Nash equilibrium.

But wait: if both players are rational and the game is symmetric, the equilibrium might be symmetric — both use the same strategy.

In that case, at symmetric equilibrium, each player’s strategy is the same, and no one wants to deviate.

So let’s try to find a **symmetric mixed strategy Nash equilibrium**.

Let’s suppose both players use the same mixed strategy q = [q0, q1, q2, q3] = prob(A0=2), A1=3, A2=4, A3=5.

Then, for the strategy to be a Nash equilibrium, every pure strategy that has positive probability in q must give the same expected payoff, and all others must be no better.

So we’ll try to find q such that:

- For any two actions i and j with q_i > 0 and q_j > 0, the expected payoff from playing i vs. q equals that from j vs. q.

Let’s denote q0 = p2, q1 = p3, q2 = p4, q3 = p5, and p2+p3+p4+p5=1.

Let’s suppose all four actions have positive probability in equilibrium. Then their expected payoffs must be equal.

Let’s define the expected payoff to you if you play action X when opponent plays mixed strategy q:

E_X = Σ_{Y=2}^5 q_Y * f(X,Y)

We want E_2 = E_3 = E_4 = E_5

Let’s compute E_X:

Let:
- q2 = a
- q3 = b
- q4 = c
- q5 = d

With a + b + c + d = 1

Then:

E_2 = a * f(2,2) + b * f(2,3) + c * f(2,4) + d * f(2,5)  
= a * 2 + b * 4 + c * 4 + d * 4  
= 2a + 4b + 4c + 4d

Similarly:

E_3 = a * f(3,2) + b * f(3,3) + c * f(3,4) + d * f(3,5)  
= a * 0 + b * 3 + c * 5 + d * 5  
= 3b + 5c + 5d

E_4 = a * f(4,2) + b * f(4,3) + c * f(4,4) + d * f(4,5)  
= a * 0 + b * 1 + c * 4 + d * 6  
= b + 4c + 6d

E_5 = a * f(5,2) + b * f(5,3) + c * f(5,4) + d * f(5,5)  
= a * 0 + b * 1 + c * 2 + d * 5  
= b + 2c + 5d

Set all equal:

E_2 = E_3 = E_4 = E_5 = E

So:
1. 2a + 4b + 4c + 4d = E
2. 3b + 5c + 5d = E
3. b + 4c + 6d = E
4. b + 2c + 5d = E

Also: a + b + c + d = 1

Now solve this system.

Start with equations 3 and 4:

Eq3: b + 4c + 6d = E  
Eq4: b + 2c + 5d = E

Subtract eq4 from eq3:

(4c - 2c) + (6d - 5d) = 0 → 2c + d = 0

So: 2c + d = 0 → d = -2c

But c, d ≥ 0 → d = -2c ≥ 0 → only possible if c = d = 0

Then d = 0, c = 0

Then from eq4: b + 0 + 0 = E → b = E

From eq2: 3b + 0 + 0 = E → 3b = E → so b = E → 3b = b → 2b = 0 → b = 0

Then E = 0

From eq1: 2a + 0 + 0 + 0 = E = 0 → a = 0

So a = b = c = d = 0 → contradiction (sum is 0 ≠ 1)

So **no solution with all four actions having positive probability**

Therefore, **in equilibrium, not all actions are used**.

So we need to try subsets.

### **Step 4: Try Simplified Strategies**

### **Step 2: Consider strategic implications**

Let’s examine what happens in various cases.

#### Case: You pick X, opponent picks Y

| X vs Y | You get | Opponent gets |
|--------|--------|---------------|
| X = Y | X | Y |
| X < Y | X + 2 | X - 2 |
| X > Y | Y - 2 | Y + 2 |

Important observations:
- When you **lose** (X < Y), you gain +2, but opponent loses 2 → this is *very* good for you.
- When you **win** (X > Y), you get Y - 2 (so you gain less than Y), and opponent gains Y + 2 → this is *bad* for you.
- When you tie (X = Y), you both get X.

So:
- **You benefit when you lose by a small margin** (e.g., you play 3, opponent plays 4 → you get 5, they get 1).
- **You lose badly when you win** (e.g., you play 5, opponent plays 3 → you get 1, they get 5).
- **Tying gives you X**, but might not be optimal if you can benefit from losing.

- **Losing (X < Y)** gives you a **bonus**: X + 2 → this is high if X is big.

But we want to **maximize worst-case** or **expected**.

Let’s suppose we consider **strict dominance**.

Is A0 strictly dominated by another action?

Is there an action that always gives higher payoff?

No — A0 gives 2 if opponent plays 2, but A1 gives 0 → so A1 is worse in that case.

So A0 is not strictly dominated.

But let’s compare A0 vs A2 (4):

A0 gives:
- 2 if opp plays 2
- 4 if opp plays 3,4,5

A2 gives:
- 2 - 2 = 0 if opp plays 2
- 3 - 2 = 1 if opp plays 3
- 4 if tie
- 4 + 2 = 6 if opp plays 5

So:
- Opp plays 2: A0 gives 2, A2 gives 0 → A0 better
- Opp plays 3: A0: 4, A2: 1 → A0 better
- Opp plays 4: A0: 4, A2: 4 → same
- Opp plays 5: A0: 4, A2: 6 → A2 better

So A0 is better when opp plays 2,3,4; A2 better when opp plays 5.

So neither dominates.

Now try **A3 (5)**:

Payoff for A3 (5):
- Opp plays 2: win → 2 - 2 = 0
- Opp plays 3: win → 3 - 2 = 1
- Opp plays 4: win → 4 - 2 = 2
- Opp plays 5: tie → 5

So: 0,1,2,5 → average depends on opponent.

Compare to A2 (4):
- Opp plays 2: win → 0
- Opp plays 3: win → 1
- Opp plays 4: tie → 4
- Opp plays 5: lose → 6

So:
- Opp plays 2: both 0 → same
- Opp plays 3: both 1 → same
- Opp plays 4: A2 gets 4, A3 gets 2 → A2 better
- Opp plays 5: A2 gets 6, A3 gets 5 → A2 better

So **A2 dominates A3** in all cases.

Even better: A2 gives at least as much as A3 in every outcome, and strictly more when opponent plays 4 or 5.

So **A3 is strictly dominated by A2**.

Therefore, **we should never play A3 (5)**.

Remove A3 from consideration.

Now the game is over A0, A1, A2.

Now compare A2 vs A1:

A2 (4):
- Opp plays 2: win → 0
- Opp plays 3: win → 1
- Opp plays 4: tie → 4
- Opp plays 5: lose → 6

A1 (3):
- Opp plays 2: win → 0
- Opp plays 3: tie → 3
- Opp plays 4: lose → 5
- Opp plays 5: lose → 5

So:
- Opp plays 2: same (0)
- Opp plays 3: A2: 1, A1: 3 → A1 better
- Opp plays 4: A2: 4, A1: 5 → A1 better
- Opp plays 5: A2: 6, A1: 5 → A2 better

So A1 is better when opp plays 3 or 4, A2 better when opp plays 5.

Now A0 (2):
- Opp plays 2: tie → 2
- Opp plays 3: lose → 4
- Opp plays 4: lose → 4
- Opp plays 5: lose → 4

So A0:
- Opp 2: 2
- Opp 3/4/5: 4

Compare to A1:
- Opp 2: 0
- Opp 3: 3
- Opp 4: 5
- Opp 5: 5

So A0 better when opp plays 2,3,4 → only A1 better when opp plays 5.

Wait:
- Opp 2: A0:2, A1:0 → A0 better
- Opp 3: A0:4, A1:3 → A0 better
- Opp 4: A0:4, A1:5 → A1 better
- Opp 5: A0:4, A1:5 → A1 better

So A1 better when opp plays 4 or 5; A0 better when opp plays 2 or 3.

Now, we know A3 is dominated → so we won't play it.

Now, is there a mixed strategy?

Let’s suppose both players are rational and symmetric. Then we can look for symmetric Nash equilibrium.

But we only care about maximizing our own payoff.

Let’s suppose we play a mixed strategy over A0, A1, A2.

Let’s denote our strategy: (p0, p1, p2), with p3 = 0.

Opponent also chooses a strategy (q0, q1, q2), q3 = 0.

We want to find a strategy that maximizes expected payoff against any best reply.

Alternatively, look for **mutual best response**.

Let me assume that **the opponent is also playing a symmetric strategy**, so we search for a symmetric Nash equilibrium.

But we can also simulate.

Key insight: Since **A2 gives higher payoff when opponent plays 5**, and **A0 gives high payoff when opponent plays 2,3,4**, maybe there's a mix.

But the real power is in **getting the opponent to play 5**, so that you get X+2 from a big X.

But if the opponent plays 5, you get:
- If you play A0: 4
- A1: 5
- A2: 6
- A3: 5 (but we know A3 is dominated → so not optimal for opponent to play A3)

So if opponent plays 5, you should play A2 to get 6.

But if opponent plays 5, they get 5 if tie, or 3 if you play less (since Y = 5, X < Y: opponent gets X - 2. So if you play A0 (2), op gets 0; if A1 (3), op gets 1; if A2 (4), op gets 2; if A3 (5), op gets 5.

So **opponent gets more when they tie** or when you play low.

But if they play 5:
- If you play A2, they get 4 - 2 = 2
- If you play A1, they get 3 - 2 = 1
- If you play A0, they get 2 - 2 = 0
- If you play A3, they get 5

So they get **maximum 5** only if you also play 5.

But if **you play 5**, they get 5, but you get 5.

But we know **A3 is dominated**, so if opponent plays A3, they are making a mistake, because A2 gives them better payoffs.

So opponent will **not play A3**.

Similarly, **we should not play A3**.

So **both players will play only A0, A1, A2**.

Now, let’s suppose opponent plays only A2 (4) with probability 1.

Then we:
- Play A0: lose → get 2 + 2 = 4
- Play A1: lose → get 3 + 2 = 5
- Play A2: tie → get 4

So best is A1 → 5

But if opponent plays A1 (3):
- We play A0: win → 2 - 2 = 0
- A1: tie → 3
- A2: win → 3 - 2 = 1

So best is A1 → 3

If opponent plays A0 (2):
- We play A0: tie → 2
- A1: win → 2 - 2 = 0
- A2: win → 2 - 2 = 0

So best is A0 → 2

So payoff depends on opponent.

Now, try to find **equilibrium**.

Let’s consider if **both players play A2 with 100%**.

Then:
- You play 4, opp plays 4 → tie → you get 4.

Is this stable?

If you deviate and play A1: you play 3, opp plays 4 → you lose → you get 3 + 2 = 5 > 4 → so better to deviate.

So (A2, A2) is not equilibrium.

Now try: suppose both play A1 (3).

You get 3.

If you deviate to A2: play 4, opp plays 3 → you win → you get 3 - 2 = 1 < 3 → worse.

If you deviate to A0: play 2, opp plays 3 → you lose → you get 2 + 2 = 4 > 3 → better.

So you want to deviate → not equilibrium.

Now try both play A0 (2): you get 2.

Deviate to A1: you get 0 (since opp plays 2, you win → 2 - 2 = 0) < 2 → worse.

Deviate to A2: win → 0 → worse.

So only A0 gives 2, others give less → so A0 is best reply? But wait:

If you play A0, you get 2.

But if you play A1, you get 0 → worse.

A2: win → 0 → worse.

So A0 is best reply to A0.

But is that stable?

Wait: if both play A0, you both get 2.

But if you play A2 → you win (2 < 2? No, X=4 > Y=2 → you win → you get Y - 2 = 0 → worse than 2.

Oh! Wait: if you play A2 (4), opponent plays A0 (2), then X=4 > Y=2 → you win → you get Y - 2 = 2 - 2 = 0 → worse than 2.

Similarly, A1: win → 2 - 2 = 0.

So only A0 gives 2.

So against A0, A0 is best strategy → so (A0,A0) is a pure strategy equilibrium?

But wait — what about if opponent plays A0, and you play A1: you win → X=3 > Y=2 → you get Y-2=0.

Yes.

But is this **only** equilibrium?

But it’s not very good — only 2 points.

Now, earlier we saw that if you play A0, you get:
- 2 if opp plays 2
- 4 if opp plays 3,4,5

But A1 gives you:
- 0 if opp plays 2
- 5 if opp plays 4 or 5
- 3 if tie

So if opponent plays 4 or 5 with high probability, A1 is better.

But opponent might not do that.

Let’s suppose we consider a **mixed strategy**.

Let’s suppose both players use **uniform strategy over A0, A1, A2** (excluding A3).

So p0 = p1 = p2 = 1/3

Then your expected payoff?

Let’s compute.

Your action: random with prob 1/3 each.

Opponent: same.

We compute E[ payoff ] for you.

We need to compute over all 9 combinations.

| You \ Opp | A0 (2) | A1 (3) | A2 (4) |
|-----------|--------|--------|--------|
| A0 (2)   | tie:2  | you lo: 2+2=4 | you lo:4 |
| A1 (3)   | you win:3-2=1 | tie:3 | you lo:3+2=5 |
| A2 (4)   | you win:2-2=0 | you win:3-2=1 | tie:4 |

Each cell has probability (1/3)*(1/3) = 1/9.

Now sum up your payoff:

- (A0,A0): 2 → 2 * 1/9 = 2/9
- (A0,A1): 4 → 4/9
- (A0,A2): 4 → 4/9
- (A1,A0): 1 → 1/9
- (A1,A1): 3 → 3/9
- (A1,A2): 5 → 5/9
- (A2,A0): 0 → 0/9
- (A2,A1): 1 → 1/9
- (A2,A2): 4 → 4/9

Now total expected payoff:

(2 + 4 + 4 + 1 + 3 + 5 + 0 + 1 + 4)/9 = (24)/9 ≈ 2.666...

But wait: is this the best?

Can we do better?

Let’s suppose we try to play **A2 with high probability**, so we can get 6 when opponent plays 5.

But opponent won’t play 5.

Wait, but we know **A3 is dominated**, so probably not played.

But in equilibrium, if both players are rational, they won’t play A3.

But maybe they play A0 or A1.

Let’s suppose we **deviate from uniform** and play A2 more.

Try: play A2 with 50%, A1 with 50%, A0 with 0.

So (0, 0.5, 0.5, 0)

But A3 is 0.

Now compute expected payoff if opponent plays (0,0.5,0.5)

Then for you:

- You play A1 (3): with prob 0.5
  - Opp A1 (3): tie → 3 → prob 0.5 → contrib: 3 * 0.5 * 0.5 = 0.75
  - Opp A2 (4): you lose → 3 + 2 = 5 → prob 0.5 → contrib: 5 * 0.5 * 0.5 = 1.25
  - Total from A1: 0.75 + 1.25 = 2

- You play A2 (4): with prob 0.5
  - Opp A1 (3): you win → 3 - 2 = 1 → 1 * 0.5 * 0.5 = 0.25
  - Opp A2 (4): tie → 4 → 4 * 0.5 * 0.5 = 1
  - Total: 1.25

- Total expected payoff: 2 + 1.25 = 3.25

Better than 2.66.

Now try: play more A2.

Try: A2: 60%, A1: 40%, A0: 0

Then your expected payoff against opponent (A1:40%, A2:60%)

Compute:

You play A1 (3), prob 0.4:

- Opp A1: tie → 3, prob 0.4 → contrib: 3 * 0.4 * 0.4 = 0.48
- Opp A2: lose → 3+2=5, prob 0.6 → contrib: 5 * 0.4 * 0.6 = 1.2
- Total: 0.48 + 1.2 = 1.68

You play A2 (4), prob 0.6:

- Opp A1: you win → 3-2=1, prob 0.4 → 1 * 0.6 * 0.4 = 0.24
- Opp A2: tie → 4, prob 0.6 → 4 * 0.6 * 0.6 = 1.44
- Total: 0.24 + 1.44 = 1.68

Total expected: 1.68 + 1.68 = 3.36

Better.

- Total A1: 1.32

- Opp A1: win → 1 * 0.7*0.3 = 0.21
- Opp A2: tie → 4 * 0.7*0.7 = 1.96
- Total A2: 2.17

Better.

A2: 0.8

If opponent plays A1 (3): you win → you get 1, opponent gets 3-2=1

If opponent plays A2: tie → you get 4, opponent gets 4

So the only way to have equilibrium is if both players are indifferent between actions.

Let’s suppose that in equilibrium, both play A0, A1, A2 with some probabilities.

Let’s assume symmetric equilibrium.

Let p0, p1, p2, p3 be the opponent's strategy, p3=0.

We want to find a strategy such that **no player wants to deviate**.

But we only care about our own payoff.

Let’s suppose we play a mixed strategy, and the opponent plays the same, and we are indifferent between all actions.

Suppose that in equilibrium, the expected payoff of playing A0, A1, A2 are all equal.

Let’s denote E0, E1, E2 as expected payoff of playing A0, A1, A2 respectively, given opponent's strategy.

For equilibrium, we want E0 = E1 = E2.

Let opponent play:
- p0: prob play A0 (2)
- p1: play A1 (3)
- p2: play A2 (4)
- p3: 0

Now compute E0 (your payoff if you play A0):

- Opp plays A0: tie → 2, prob p0 → contrib: 2 * p0
- Opp plays A1: you lose → 2+2=4, prob p1 → 4 * p1
- Opp plays A2: you lose → 4, prob p2 → 4 * p2
- Total E0 = 2p0 + 4p1 + 4p2

E1 (you play A1):

- Opp A0: you win → 2-2=0, prob p0 → 0*p0
- Opp A1: tie → 3, prob p1 → 3*p1
- Opp A2: you lose → 3+2=5, prob p2 → 5*p2
- Total E1 = 0*p0 + 3p1 + 5p2

E2 (you play A2):

- Opp A0: you win → 2-2=0, prob p0 → 0
- Opp A1: you win → 3-2=1, prob p1 → 1*p1
- Opp A2: tie → 4, prob p2 → 4*p2
- Total E2 = 0 + p1 + 4p2

Set E0 = E1 = E2

And p0 + p1 + p2 = 1

Let’s set E0 = E1:

2p0 + 4p1 + 4p2 = 3p1 + 5p2

Simplify:

2p0 + 4p1 + 4p2 - 3p1 - 5p2 = 0 → 2p0 + p1 - p2 = 0

→ 2p0 + p1 = p2  ...(1)

Set E1 = E2:

3p1 + 5p2 = p1 + 4p2

→ 3p1 + 5p2 - p1 - 4p2 = 0 → 2p1 + p2 = 0

But p1, p2 >= 0, so 2p1 + p2 = 0 → p1=0, p2=0

Then from (1): 2p0 = 0 → p0=0

Contradiction (no strategy).

So cannot have all three expected payoffs equal.

That means **no symmetric equilibrium with positive support on A0,A1,A2**.

But A3 is dominated, so not in support.

===== Response [ID: 5a19aba5] [2026-01-25 10:06:38] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's carefully analyze this game step by step to determine the optimal mixed strategy (probability distribution over actions) for Player 1, aiming to **maximize expected payoff**.

---

### **Step 1: Understand the actions and values**

- A0 → 2  
- A1 → 3  
- A2 → 4  
- A3 → 5  

So, each action corresponds to a fixed number:  
X ∈ {2, 3, 4, 5}  
Y ∈ {2, 3, 4, 5}

---

### **Step 2: Understand the payoff rules**

Let’s denote:
- X = your chosen number (from your action)
- Y = opponent’s chosen number (from their action)

Payoffs:
- **If X = Y**: You get X, opponent gets Y → both get X (same)
- **If X < Y**: You get X + 2.0, opponent gets X - 2.0  
- **If X > Y**: You get Y - 2.0, opponent gets Y + 2.0

Note: Payoffs depend on the actual numbers, not the action names.

Let’s rephrase this in terms of the values:

| Relative Order | Your Payoff | Opponent’s Payoff |
|----------------|-------------|-------------------|
| X = Y          | X           | Y (=X)            |
| X < Y          | X + 2       | X - 2             |
| X > Y          | Y - 2       | Y + 2             |

Key observation:  
- When you **lose** (X < Y), you gain an extra 2, but opponent loses 2.  
- When you **win** (X > Y), you lose 2, but opponent gains 2.  
- When **tie**, both get the number.

But here’s a **critical point**: When X < Y, you get **X + 2**, which is **better than the tie payoff** (which is just X). So **losing in terms of number comparison gives you a +2 payoff**, which is actually **good**.

Similarly, when X > Y, you get Y - 2 — **worse** than what you'd get in a tie (which would be Y). So **winning is bad in terms of payoff**.

---

### **Step 3: Analyze incentives**

Let’s analyze what each outcome gives you:

| Your X | Opponent Y | Outcome | Your Payoff |
|--------|------------|--------|-------------|
| 2      | 2          | Tie    | 2           |
| 2      | 3          | X < Y  | 2 + 2 = 4   |
| 2      | 4          | X < Y  | 2 + 2 = 4   |
| 2      | 5          | X < Y  | 2 + 2 = 4   |

→ So if you play 2, no matter what opponent plays, you get **at least 4** (since 2 < 3, 4, 5 → always X < Y), so you get **4**.

Now try X = 3:
- Y = 2 → X > Y → your payoff: Y - 2 = 2 - 2 = 0
- Y = 3 → tie → 3
- Y = 4 → X < Y → 3 + 2 = 5
- Y = 5 → X < Y → 3 + 2 = 5

So if you play 3:
- If opponent plays 2 → you get 0
- Others: 3 or 5

X = 4:
- Y = 2 → X > Y → Y - 2 = 0
- Y = 3 → X > Y → 3 - 2 = 1
- Y = 4 → tie → 4
- Y = 5 → X < Y → 4 + 2 = 6

X = 5:
- Y = 2 → X > Y → Y - 2 = 0
- Y = 3 → X > Y → 3 - 2 = 1
- Y = 4 → X > Y → 4 - 2 = 2
- Y = 5 → tie → 5

So let's tabulate your **payoff** for each of your action vs opponent's:

|      | Y=2 | Y=3 | Y=4 | Y=5 |
|------|-----|-----|-----|-----|
| X=2  | 4   | 4   | 4   | 4   |
| X=3  | 0   | 3   | 5   | 5   |
| X=4  | 0   | 1   | 4   | 6   |
| X=5  | 0   | 1   | 2   | 5   |

This table is **your expected payoff per outcome**.

Now, this is a **simultaneous-move game**, and we're trying to find a **mixed strategy** for Player 1 (you) to maximize your **expected payoff**, **assuming the opponent is rational and also optimizing their own payoff**.

But here’s a twist: **The game is not symmetric**, because your payoff depends on *your* and *opponent’s* values, but also the comparison. Also, when you “lose” (X < Y), you get a **bonus**, and when you “win” (X > Y), you get a **penalty**.

This is a **non-standard** incentive structure — unlike typical games where winning is better.

Let’s analyze: **The best outcomes for you occur when you have a lower number than the opponent**, because then you get X + 2.

In particular, **playing A0 (X=2)** gives you **4** in **all cases**, regardless of opponent’s choice.

That’s a **constant payoff of 4**, no matter what.

Compare to other actions:

- A1 (X=3): Payoffs depend: gets 0, 3, 5, 5 → but if opponent plays 2, you get only 0.
- A2 (X=4): 0,1,4,6 — worst if opponent plays 2 or 3.
- A3 (X=5): 0,1,2,5

So **if opponent plays 2**, playing anything above 2 gives you 0 — very bad.

But if opponent plays 2 with **high probability**, then playing 2 is best — because you get 4 regardless.

But opponent might also be rational.

Let’s consider **iterated reasoning**.

---

### **Step 4: Is there a dominated strategy?**

Let’s check if any action is **strictly dominated**.

Compare A0 (X=2) → always gives 4

Compare to A3 (X=5):  
- if opponent plays 2, you get 0 → worse than 4  
- if opponent plays 3, you get 1 → worse than 4  
- if opponent plays 4, get 2 → worse  
- if opponent plays 5, get 5 → better than 4

But only **better when opponent plays 5**.

Similarly, A2: gets 0 when Y=2, 1 when Y=3, 4 when Y=4, 6 when Y=5 → good when opponent is high, bad when lower.

So **A0 is never worse than any other action**? Let’s see.

Is there a case where playing 2 gives less than some other action?

No — always gets 4.

Now consider A1 (X=3):  
- When Y=2 → you get 0 < 4 → worse  
- Y=3 → 3 < 4 → worse  
- Y=4 or 5 → 5 > 4 → better  
So if opponent plays 4 or 5, A1 beats A0.

But if opponent plays 2 or 3, A0 is better.

So **A0 is not strictly dominated**, but **not dominant either**.

So no strict dominance.

But here's a key idea: **Playing A0 gives a guaranteed 4**, no matter what the opponent does.

Now suppose the opponent is playing optimally. What would they do?

Let’s reverse engineer.

### **Step 5: Consider opponent’s perspective (Player 2)**

From opponent's perspective:
- Their payoff is symmetric in form:
    - If X = Y: they get Y
    - If X < Y: they get X - 2
    - If X > Y: they get Y + 2

But note: for player 2, **if they play a low number and X > Y**, they get Y + 2 → good.

Also, if X < Y, they get X - 2 → bad.

So player 2 **wants to play low numbers** to trigger X > Y and get Y + 2.

So rational player 2 will **want to play low numbers** (A0 or A1) to get +2 when opponent is higher.

- if X=2 → tie → payoff = 2

- X=3 → X < Y → 3 - 2 = 1
- X=4 → tie → 4
- X=5 → X > Y → you get 4 + 2 = 6

- X=2 → X < Y → you get 2 - 2 = 0

A0 (Y=2):
- X=2 → tie → 2
- X=3,4,5 → X > Y → you get 2 + 2 = 4

So only when X=2 do you get 2; otherwise 4

So opponent’s options:
- A0: gets 4 if X > 2 → i.e., if you play 3,4,5 → gets 4
- A1: gets 5 if you play 4 or 5 → gets 5; gets 0 if you play 2
- A2: gets 6 if you play 5 → gets 6; else 0–1
- A3: gets 5 if you play 5 → otherwise 0–2

So from opponent's view, **A0** is safest: gives 4 unless you play 2 (get 2)

But **A1** gives 5 if you play 4 or 5 — better than 4

But if you play 2, A1 gives 0 — very bad

Similarly, A2 and A3 are riskier.

They prefer A0: gives 4 unless you play 2, then 2

But if they expect you to play 2, they might prefer A0 (best they can do).

But can we model this?

So your **payoff drops to 2** if opponent plays 2.

This shows the danger of playing A0: if you play A0, opponent can punish you by playing 2, resulting in tie → you get 2, not 4.

But earlier we thought you get 4 no matter what — **that was under a mistaken assumption about opponent’s choice**.

So we must model **simultaneous play** with **rational beliefs**.

### **Step 6: Look for Nash equilibrium**

Let’s suppose both players use mixed strategies.

Let your strategy be:  
P(A0) = p0, P(A1) = p1, P(A2) = p2, P(A3) = p3  
With p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = 1

---

### **Step 1: Understand the game and payoffs**

Each player chooses an action from:
- A0 → 2
- A1 → 3
- A2 → 4
- A3 → 5

You are **Player 2**.

The payoff depends on your choice X and the opponent's choice Y.

The payoff rules are:

- If X = Y:  
  - You get X points
  - Opponent gets Y = X points

- If X < Y:  
  - You get X + 2.0  
  - Opponent gets X - 2.0

- If X > Y:  
  - You get Y - 2.0  
  - Opponent gets Y + 2.0

Note: These payoffs are **not symmetric** in a way that makes cooperation or mutual equilibrium obvious.

We are to find a **mixed strategy** (probability distribution over A0–A3) that maximizes **our expected payoff**, assuming the other player is also rational and will also choose a strategy to maximize their own payoff.

This is a **two-player zero-sum game**? Let's check:

Total payoff:  
- If X = Y: X + Y = 2X → not constant  
- If X < Y: (X+2) + (X-2) = 2X  
- If X > Y: (Y-2) + (Y+2) = 2Y

So total payoff is **not constant** → not zero-sum.

But it is **not symmetric** in payoffs (you and opponent get different payouts in asymmetric cases), and the payoffs depend on relative ordering.

So we need to find a **Nash equilibrium** in mixed strategies where both players play optimally.

But remember: you're **Player 2** in the game. However, the game is **symmetric in structure** (except for player roles), but **not symmetric in payoffs** because whether you're X or Y matters.

Let’s define:
- You (player 2) pick X from {2,3,4,5}
- Opponent (player 1) picks Y from {2,3,4,5}

So we can model this as a symmetric game? Not quite — the roles are fixed: you are player 2, opponent is player 1.

But the game is symmetric in action space, so the best strategy might still be symmetric.

---

### **Step 2: Represent payoffs in a matrix**

Let’s define a payoff matrix from **your perspective (Player 2)**, where rows are your actions (X), columns are opponent’s actions (Y).

Each cell is (your payoff, opponent's payoff), but we can compute your payoff only.

Let’s compute your payoff for each (X, Y):

| X\Y | 2   | 3   | 4   | 5   |
|-----|-----|-----|-----|-----|
| 2   | 2   | 2+2=4 | 2+2=4 | 2+2=4 |
| 3   | 3-2=1 | 3   | 3+2=5 | 3+2=5 |
| 4   | 4-2=2 | 4-2=2 | 4   | 4+2=6 |
| 5   | 5-2=3 | 5-2=3 | 5-2=3 | 5   |

Wait — let’s double-check this.

#### Case-by-case:
- X = 2:
  - Y = 2: X = Y → pay: 2
  - Y = 3: X < Y → pay: X + 2 = 4
  - Y = 4: X < Y → 4
  - Y = 5: X < Y → 4

- X = 3:
  - Y = 2: X > Y → pay: Y - 2 = 2 - 2 = 0 → wait, that can’t be right.

Wait — I think I messed up the logic.

Let’s re-express the rules **very carefully**:

> If X > Y: you get Y - 2.0  
> If X < Y: you get X + 2.0  
> If X = Y: you get X

So:
- When X > Y → YOU get **Y - 2**
- When X < Y → YOU get **X + 2**
- When X = Y → YOU get **X**

So for **you**, the payoff is:
- If X > Y: Y - 2
- If X < Y: X + 2
- If X = Y: X

Let’s recompute your payoff (Player 2) matrix:

| X (your choice)\Y (opp's choice) | 2       | 3       | 4       | 5       |
|----------------------------------|---------|---------|---------|---------|
| **2** (A0)                       | 2       | 2+2 = 4 | 2+2 = 4 | 2+2 = 4 |
| **3** (A1)                       | 2-2 = 0 | 3       | 3+2 = 5 | 3+2 = 5 |
| **4** (A2)                       | 4-2 = 2 | 4-2 = 2 | 4       | 4+2 = 6 |
| **5** (A3)                       | 5-2 = 3 | 5-2 = 3 | 5-2 = 3 | 5       |

Now fill in:
- X=3, Y=2: X > Y → you get Y - 2 = 2 - 2 = 0 ✅
- X=4, Y=3: X > Y → you get Y - 2 = 3 - 2 = 1? Wait no!

Wait a minute! **Mistake here.**

It says:  
> If X > Y: you get Y - 2.0

So **when you pick X, and X > Y**, you get **Y - 2**, not X - 2.

That’s critical.

So example:
- X=4, Y=3: X > Y → you get Y - 2 = 3 - 2 = 1
- X=4, Y=2: X > Y → you get 2 - 2 = 0
- X=5, Y=4: you get 4 - 2 = 2

I made an error earlier.

Let’s **correct the entire matrix**.

### **Correct Payoff Matrix for You (Player 2)**

| X \ Y | 2   | 3   | 4   | 5   |
|-------|-----|-----|-----|-----|
| 2 (A0) | 2   | 4   | 4   | 4   |
| 3 (A1) | 0   | 3   | 5   | 5   |
| 4 (A2) | 0   | 1   | 4   | 6   |
| 5 (A3) | 1   | 1   | 2   | 5   |

Let’s verify row by row:

- **X=2 (A0)**:
  - Y=2: X=Y → 2
  - Y=3: X<Y → X+2 = 4
  - Y=4: X<Y → 4
  - Y=5: X<Y → 4

- **X=3 (A1)**:
  - Y=2: X>Y → Y-2 = 0
  - Y=3: X=Y → 3
  - Y=4: X<Y → X+2 = 5
  - Y=5: X<Y → 5

- **X=4 (A2)**:
  - Y=2: X>Y → Y-2 = 0
  - Y=3: X>Y → Y-2 = 1
  - Y=4: X=Y → 4
  - Y=5: X<Y → X+2 = 6

- **X=5 (A3)**:
  - Y=2: X>Y → Y-2 = 0 → wait: 2-2=0
  - Y=3: X>Y → 3-2 = 1
  - Y=4: X>Y → 4-2 = 2
  - Y=5: X=Y → 5

👉 But wait: Y=2 → 2-2=0? So X=5, Y=2: you get 0?

Yes.

So corrected row for X=5:
- Y=2: 0
- Y=3: 1
- Y=4: 2
- Y=5: 5

But earlier I said 1,1,2,5 — yes, correct.

So final matrix:

| X \ Y | 2   | 3   | 4   | 5   |
|-------|-----|-----|-----|-----|
| A0 (2) | 2   | 4   | 4   | 4   |
| A1 (3) | 0   | 3   | 5   | 5   |
| A2 (4) | 0   | 1   | 4   | 6   |
| A3 (5) | 0   | 1   | 2   | 5   |

Now, the other player (Player 1) receives different payoffs, but since we are only optimizing **our own** payoff, we can focus on **this matrix**.

We want to find a **mixed strategy** (probability distribution over A0–A3) that maximizes **your expected payoff**, assuming your opponent is also playing optimally (i.e., in Nash equilibrium).

Because it's a **finite two-player game**, a Nash equilibrium exists. We are to find a symmetric or asymmetric equilibrium, but since the game is symmetric in action space, the equilibrium might be symmetric.

But note: **the payoffs are not symmetric**, because when Y is small, you benefit less when you lose.

For example, if you pick X=5 and opponent picks Y=2, you lose big — you get only 0.

But if you pick X=2 and opponent picks Y=3, you get 4 — good!

So the game is **not symmetric** in payoffs for the two players, but structure is symmetric.

So likely, equilibrium will be **symmetric in strategy**.

Let’s assume both players use the same mixed strategy: p = (p0, p1, p2, p3) for A0–A3.

Then, your expected payoff when you play A0 is:
E0 = 2*p0 + 4*p1 + 4*p2 + 4*p3

Similarly:
E1 = 0*p0 + 3*p1 + 5*p2 + 5*p3  
E2 = 0*p0 + 1*p1 + 4*p2 + 6*p3  
E3 = 0*p0 + 1*p1 + 2*p2 + 5*p3

In equilibrium, all **pure strategies that are played with positive probability** must have the same expected payoff, and no strategy outside the support should yield higher payoff.

So we assume a **fully mixed equilibrium** (all actions used), or try smaller supports.

Try to find which strategies are in support.

Look at the matrix:
- A0 (X=2) gives 2 if Y=2, else 4
- A1 (X=3): 0 vs Y=2, 3 vs Y=3, 5 vs Y=4,5
- A3 (X=5): 0 vs Y=2, 1 vs Y=3, 2 vs Y=4, 5 vs Y=5

Notice that **A0 gives at least 2**, and better against higher Y.

A3 is very bad when Y=2 or 3.

A1 is bad when Y=2.

But the real danger for you is playing high X when opponent plays low Y → you get Y-2 → small.

Similarly, if you play low X and opponent plays high Y → you get X+2 → good.

So there's a motive to **play low numbers** when expecting opponent to play high.

But let’s test for **dominated strategies**.

#### Is A0 dominated?

Compare A0: payoffs [2,4,4,4]

A1: [0,3,5,5] → worse than A0 when Y=2 (0<2), better when Y≥3

A2: [0,1,4,6] → worse at Y=2,3; better at Y=4,5

A3: [0,1,2,5] → worse on all but Y=5

So **A0 is not dominated**.

But maybe A3 is weak.

Compare A3 to A1: A1 does better for Y=3 (3 vs 1), Y=4 (5 vs 2), Y=5 (5 vs 5), Y=2 (0 vs 0)

A1 vs A3: A1 ≥ A3 for all Y, and strictly better for Y=3,4

So A3 is **strictly dominated** by A1?

- Y=2: both 0 → equal
- Y=3: 1 vs 3 → A1 better
- Y=4: 2 vs 5 → A1 better
- Y=5: 5 vs 5 → equal

So A1 dominates A3 in all cases, and strictly better when Y=3 or Y=4.

Thus, **A3 (5) is strictly dominated by A1 (3)**.

Therefore, in any rational strategy, you **should never play A3**, because A1 is always at least as good, and better in half the cases.

So **we can eliminate A3** from the support.

Similarly, let's check A2.

Compare A2: [0,1,4,6]

A1: [0,3,5,5]

- Y=2: 0 vs 0 → equal
- Y=3: 1 vs 3 → A1 better
- Y=4: 4 vs 5 → A1 better
- Y=5: 6 vs 5 → A2 better

So A1 is better except when Y=5.

But A2 is better when Y=5.

So A2 not dominated by A1.

Compare A0: [2,4,4,4] vs A2: [0,1,4,6]

- Y=2: 2 vs 0 → A0 better
- Y=3: 4 vs 1 → A0 better
- Y=4: 4 vs 4 → equal
- Y=5: 4 vs 6 → A2 better

So A0 better except Y=5.

Now increase weights.

But we already eliminated A3.

Now, is A2 dominated?

Suppose we consider only A0, A1, A2.

Check if any is dominated.

We suspect that **A2** might be played only if you expect opponent to play high.

Similarly, A0 is good when opponent plays high.

But now focus: **A3 is strictly dominated**, so we should **never play A3**.

So best to set **p3 = 0**.

Now, suppose opponent also plays optimally — will they ever play A3?

Let’s look at what the opponent wants.

If opponent (player 1) plays Y=5, and you (player 2) play X=2: you get 4 → good
But if you play X=5, you get 5 → also good, but only if Y=5.

But if you play X=3, Y=5: you get 5 → same.

But if you play X=3, Y=2: you get 0

So opponent has an incentive to play low if you play high.

But from opponent’s perspective: when they play Y=2 (A0), they get:
- If X=2: Y=2 → 2
- X=3: X>Y → opponent gets Y+2 = 4
- X=4: Y+2 = 4
- X=5: Y+2 = 4

So opponent gets 2 if you play 2, 4 otherwise → so opponent **likes** you to play high.

Similarly, if opponent plays Y=3:
- X=2: X<Y → opponent gets X-2 = 0
- X=3: Y → 3
- X=4: X>Y → opponent gets Y+2 = 5
- X=5: X>Y → opponent gets 5

So opponent gets 0 if you play 2, 3 if you play 3, 5 otherwise.

So opponent **hates** you playing X=2.

So opponent wants you to play high.

But if you play X=2, you get 4 if Y≥3, so you like opponent to play high.

So there is **a conflict of interest**.

But back to our strategy.

Since **A3 is strictly dominated by A1**, and this is true **regardless** of opponent’s strategy, we can safely **never play A3**.

So p3 = 0.

Now, can we eliminate any other?

Let’s try to solve for a **mixed strategy equilibrium** over A0, A1, A2 only.

Let p = (p0, p1, p2, 0) with p0 + p1 + p2 = 1.

We want that **all strategies in support give same expected payoff**, and no better.

Try support {A0, A1, A2}

Let’s suppose optimal strategy uses A0, A1, A2 with probs p0, p1, p2.

Then for each, expected payoff must be equal.

Let E be the common expected payoff.

Compute expected payoff from playing:
- A0: 2*p0 + 4*p1 + 4*p2
- A1: 0*p0 + 3*p1 + 5*p2
- A2: 0*p0 + 1*p1 + 4*p2

Set them equal:

(1) 2p0 + 4p1 + 4p2 = E  
(2) 0p0 + 3p1 + 5p2 = E  
(3) 0p0 + 1p1 + 4p2 = E  
(4) p0 + p1 + p2 = 1

Use (2) and (3):

From (2): E = 3p1 + 5p2  
From (3): E = p1 + 4p2

Set equal:
3p1 + 5p2 = p1 + 4p2  
→ 2p1 + p2 = 0 → p1 = -p2/2

But probabilities can’t be negative → impossible.

So no solution with all three A0, A1, A2 in support.

So cannot have all three.

Now, try support with only two strategies.

Try **A0 and A1**

Then p2 = 0, p3 = 0, so p0 + p1 = 1

Then:
- E(A0) = 2p0 + 4p1
- E(A1) = 0p0 + 3p1

Set equal:
2p0 + 4p1 = 3p1  
→ 2p0 + p1 = 0 → p0 = -p1/2 → impossible

No solution.

Try **A0 and A2**

p1 = 0, p3 = 0 → p0 + p2 = 1

E(A0) = 2p0 + 4p2  
E(A2) = 0p0 + 1*0 + 4p2 = 4p2

Set equal:
2p0 + 4p2 = 4p2 → 2p0 = 0 → p0 = 0

Then p2 = 1

So only A2 played.

If you play A2, and opponent plays A2: you get 4

Same as playing A2.

- You play A2: X=4, Y=2 → X>Y → you get Y-2 = 0
- You play A0: X=2, Y=2 → you get 2

So better to play A0 if opponent plays A0.

But if opponent plays A2, then A0 gives 4, A2 gives 4.

But if opponent plays A2 always, then:
- A0: 4
- A2: 4
- A1: 5? Wait: A1 vs Y=2: X=3>Y=2 → you get Y-2=0 → worse
- A3: 0

So if opponent plays A2 only, best response is A0 or A2 (both give 4)

So **A2 alone** is not a best response if opponent plays A2 — A0 is equally good.

But if you play A0, your opponent knows you play A0, they can play Y=2 → you get 2, or Y=3 → you get 4, etc.

Wait — we need Nash equilibrium.

Suppose opponent plays only A2 → then you get:
- A0: 4
- A1: 0
- A2: 4
- A3: 0

So best responses: A0 and A2

So if you play a mixture, but opponent is fixed.

Your expected payoff depends on opponent's strategy.

In equilibrium, opponent should play a best response.

- A2: 0
- A3: 0

Conflict.

- If X > Y → you get Y - 2  

Correct payoff matrix (Player 1):

| You \ Opp | A0 (2) | A1 (3) | A2 (4) | A3 (5) |
|-----------|--------|--------|--------|--------|
| **A0 (2)** | 2      | 4      | 4      | 4      |
| **A1 (3)** | 0      | 3      | 5      | 5      |
| **A2 (4)** | 0      | 1      | 4      | 6      |
| **A3 (5)** | 0      | 1      | 2      | 5      |

This matrix shows that:
- Playing **A0** gives you **4** whenever the opponent plays A1, A2, or A3 (since you are lower), and **2** if they play A0.
- Playing **A3** gives you **0** if they play A0, **1** if A1, **2** if A2, and **5** if A3 → only good if they play A3.
- **A2** is strong when opponent plays A3 (gets 6), but weak if they play lower.

Observation: **A0 gives consistent 4 if opponent plays anything above 2** (i.e., A1, A2, A3), but only 2 if they also play A0.

**A3** is risky: bad unless opponent also plays A3.

**A1** is weak against A0 and A2: gives 0 or 1.

But **A2** is strong against A3 (6), not bad elsewhere.

### **Step 2: Look for symmetric Nash equilibrium using support {A0, A2, A3}**

Guess: Rational players may avoid A1 because it's weak in almost all cases.

Let opponent play:  
- A0 with probability **p**  
- A2 with probability **q**  
- A3 with probability **r**  
With **p + q + r = 1**, and **p, q, r ≥ 0**

In Nash equilibrium, **each action in support must give equal expected payoff**.

Compute expected payoff for each action **against opponent’s strategy**:

#### E(A0) = p*2 + q*4 + r*4 = 2p + 4q + 4r

#### E(A2) = p*0 + q*4 + r*6 = 0p + 4q + 6r

#### E(A3) = p*0 + q*2 + r*5 = 0p + 2q + 5r

Set them equal:

**E(A0) = E(A2):**  
2p + 4q + 4r = 4q + 6r  
=> 2p + 4q + 4r - 4q - 6r = 0  
=> 2p - 2r = 0 → p = r   → (Eq1)

**E(A2) = E(A3):**  
4q + 6r = 2q + 5r  
=> 4q - 2q + 6r - 5r = 0  
=> 2q + r = 0 → (Eq2)

But q ≥ 0, r ≥ 0 → 2q + r = 0 → q = r = 0 → then p = 1

Then p = 1, q = 0, r = 0 → opponent plays only A0.

Check:  
- E(A0) = 2  
- E(A2) = 0  
- E(A3) = 0  
So only A0 is best → but then you should play A0 → so in this case, playing A0 is best.

- A0: 2
- A1: 0 (since 3 > 2 → you get 2-2=0)

- A3: 0 (5 > 2 → get 0)

So best is A0 → so yes, (A0, A0) is a Nash equilibrium.
