    X=3 vs X=4: 0=0, 3>1, 5>4, 5<6. No.

    Is (2,2) the only Nash equilibrium?
    If he plays Y=2, my best response is X=2 (payoff 2).
    If I play X=2, his best response is Y=2 (payoff 2).
    So (2,2) is a Nash equilibrium.

    Are there any other Nash equilibria?
    If he plays Y=3, my payoffs for X are [4, 3, 1, 1]. Best response: X=2.
    If he plays Y=4, my payoffs for X are [4, 5, 4, 2]. Best response: X=3.
    If he plays Y=5, my payoffs for X are [4, 5, 6, 5]. Best response: X=4.

    Wait, this is the exact same structure as the classic Traveler's Dilemma.
    Actually, my matrix shows something interesting:
    If his strategy is to pick 5 (Y=5), I should pick 4.
    If his strategy is to pick 4, I should pick 3.
    If his strategy is to pick 3, I should pick 2.
    If his strategy is to pick 2, I should pick 2.

    So, if we apply the logic of iterated elimination of strictly dominated strategies, what happens?
    Wait, Row 5 is dominated by Row 4.
    X=4: [0, 1, 4, 6]
    X=5: [0, 1, 2, 5]
    Row 4 is always >= Row 5. So we can eliminate X=5.
    Once X=5 is eliminated, the other player (who is rational) will also not play Y=5.
    So we eliminate the Y=5 column.
    Now the matrix is:
          Y=2   Y=3   Y=4
    X=2 |  2  |  4  |  4  |
    X=3 |  0  |  3  |  5  |
    X=4 |  0  |  1  |  4  |

    Now, is there any dominant strategy?
    X=3 vs X=4: 0=0, 3>1, 5>4.
    X=3 weakly dominates X=4!
    So we eliminate X=4.
    Then the other player will also not play Y=4.
    The matrix becomes:
          Y=2   Y=3
    X=2 |  2  |  4  |
    X=3 |  0  |  3  |
    Now, X=2 strictly dominates X=3 (2>0 and 4>3).
    So we eliminate X=3.
    Then the other player will also not play Y=3.
    The only thing left is X=2.

    This confirmed that (2,2) is the result of iterated elimination of weakly dominated strategies.
    In game theory, this is the standard prediction for Traveler's Dilemma.

    But, I'm playing a game. Is the opponent going to play like a game theorist or a human?
    In psychological studies of Traveler's Dilemma, many players choose 5.
    If the other player chooses 5, and I choose 4, I get 6.
    If the other player chooses 5, and I choose 5, I get 5.
    If the other player chooses 5, and I choose 2, I get 4.
    If the other player chooses 4, and I choose 3, I get 5.
    If the other player chooses 4, and I choose 4, I get 4.
    If the other player chooses 4, and I choose 2, I get 4.

    This is a bit different from the standard Traveler's Dilemma because the bonus/penalty is fixed at 2, and the range of choices is very small (2, 3, 4, 5).
    In the classic version, the range is usually something like 18 to 100.
    With such a small range, the reasoning to go down to 2 is much stronger.

    Let's think. If I play 2, I am guaranteed at least 2 points, and I'll likely get 4.
    If I play 5, I might get 5 (if they also play 5) or 1 or 2.
    If I play 4, I might get 6 (if they play 5) or 4 (if they play 4) or 1 (if they play 3) or 0 (if they play 2).
    If I play 3, I might get 5 (if they play 4 or 5) or 3 (if they play 3) or 0 (if they play 2).

    Looking at the average payoffs again (assuming uniform random choice by the opponent):
    - X=2: 3.5
    - X=3: 3.25
    - X=4: 2.75
    - X=5: 2.0
    Wait, the best "blind" strategy is X=2.

    What if the other player thinks like me? They would also see that X=2 is the safest.
    If they choose X=2, then I should also choose X=2 to get 2 instead of 0.
    Is there any reason to play anything other than 2?
    Only if I'm sure the other player will play 4 or 5.
    If they play 4, X=3 gives 5, while X=2 gives 4.
    If they play 5, X=4 gives 6, X=3 gives 5, and X=2 gives 4.

    Wait, what about a mixed strategy where I play 2 mostly but also a bit of 3?
    Suppose I play 90% A0 (2) and 10% A1 (3).
    If the other player does the same:
    E(2) = 0.9*2 + 0.1*4 = 1.8 + 0.4 = 2.2
    E(3) = 0.9*0 + 0.1*3 = 0.3
    So 2 is still much better.

    What if the other player plays mostly 4s and 5s?
    If the other player plays 50% 4 and 50% 5:
    E(2) = 0.5*4 + 0.5*4 = 4
    E(3) = 0.5*5 + 0.5*5 = 5
    E(4) = 0.5*4 + 0.5*6 = 5
    E(5) = 0.5*2 + 0.5*5 = 3.5

    So if the other player is focusing on the higher numbers, I should play 3 or 4.
    But this is risky. If the other player plays even a small amount of 2s, the expected value of playing 3, 4, or 5 drops quickly.
    Let p0 be the probability that the other player plays 2.
    E(2) = 2p0 + 4(1-p0) = 4 - 2p0
    E(3) = 0p0 + 3p1 + 5p2 + 5p3 = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1

    For E(3) to be greater than E(2):
    5 - 5p0 - 2p1 > 4 - 2p0
    1 - 2p1 > 3p0
    If p0 = 0.1 (10% chance the other player plays 2), then:
    1 - 2p1 > 0.3 => 0.7 > 2p1 => p1 < 0.35.
    So if p0=0.1 and p1=0.2, then E(3) > E(2).
    Let's check this: p0=0.1, p1=0.2, p2=0.4, p3=0.3.
    E(2) = 4 - 2(0.1) = 3.8
    E(3) = 5 - 5(0.1) - 2(0.2) = 5 - 0.5 - 0.4 = 4.1
    E(4) = 6 - 6(0.1) - 5(0.2) - 2(0.4) = 6 - 0.6 - 1.0 - 0.8 = 3.6
    E(5) = 5 - 5(0.1) - 4(0.2) - 3(0.4) = 5 - 0.5 - 0.8 - 1.2 = 2.5
    In this case, E(3) is better!

    But what is the chance that the other player plays 2?
    This is the core of the dilemma. If both players are rational, they'll both play 2. If they are slightly irrational and play higher numbers, there might be a better play.
    However, in this specific game, with small choices and a +2/-2 bonus, the "2" strategy is quite strong.

    Let me consider the case where the other player is trying to be "cooperative" and play 5.
    If the other player plays 5, and I play 2, I get 4 and he gets 0.
    If we both play 5, we both get 5.
    Wait, if he plays 5, and I play 4, I get 6 and he gets 2.
    Actually, 5 is not an equilibrium. The person who plays 5 would always want to lower their number to 4. And the person who plays 4 would want to lower it to 3.

    Let's think for a moment. Is there any reason to play 5? No, because Action 4 weakly dominates Action 5. Let's look at the matrix again.
    For ANY column, Row 4 is better than or equal to Row 5:
    Row 4: [0, 1, 4, 6]
    Row 5: [0, 1, 2, 5]
    So Row 5 is definitely out.
    What about Row 4?
    Row 3: [0, 3, 5, 5]
    Row 4: [0, 1, 4, 6]
    If the other player is only playing 5, then 4 is better than 3 (6 vs 5).
    But if the other player plays 4 or 3 or 2, then 3 is better than 4.

    Is there any reason to play 3?
    Row 2: [2, 4, 4, 4]
    Row 3: [0, 3, 5, 5]
    If the other player plays 4 or 5, Row 3 is better than Row 2.
    If the other player plays 2 or 3, Row 2 is better than Row 3.

    Given the context of these games, the Nash Equilibrium (A0: 2) is often the safest and most likely choice for a rational player. However, let's see if I should diversify a little bit just in case.
    Actually, in a single-shot, the Nash equilibrium is the strongest recommendation.
    Let me consider if there is any reason to put some probability on A1 (3) or A2 (4).
    If I play 100% A0, I get:
    2.0 if they play 2
    4.0 if they play 3
    4.0 if they play 4
    4.0 if they play 5
    Average: 3.5 if they are random.

    If I play 100% A1, I get:
    0.0 if they play 2
    3.0 if they play 3
    5.0 if they play 4
    5.0 if they play 5
    Average: 3.25 if they are random.

    If I play 100% A2, I get:
    0.0 if they play 2
    1.0 if they play 3
    4.0 if they play 4
    6.0 if they play 5
    Average: 2.75 if they are random.

    Since A0 has the highest minimum and the highest average (if the other player is random), playing A0 seems very strong.

    Let me consider the possibility that both players will realize that A0 is the Nash Equilibrium.
    If both play A0, they each get 2.
    If they both could somehow cooperate and play A3 (5), they'd get 5.
    But the temptation to deviate is high (4 gives 6), and the penalty is 2.
    In small-stakes games like this, people sometimes aim for the Nash equilibrium.
    Let me think: is there any other possible reasoning?
    If I play 100% A0, I'm guaranteed at least 2.
    If I play any other strategy, I could get 0.
    For example, if I play A1, and they play A0, I get 0.
    Since this is a points-maximization game, a guaranteed 2 or 4 is quite attractive.

    Let's check if there is any mixed equilibrium I missed over {A0, A1, A2}.
    I already checked {A0, A1, A2, A3} and {A0, A1, A2}, and they all led to p0=1.
    Let's re-verify {A1, A2, A3}, assuming p0 = 0.
    E(3) = 5 - 2p1
    E(4) = 6 - 5p1 - 2p2
    E(5) = 5 - 4p1 - 3p2
    From 5-2p1 = 6-5p1-2p2: 3p1 + 2p2 = 1.
    From 6-5p1-2p2 = 5-4p1-3p2: p2 - p1 = -1 => p1 - p2 = 1.
    Substitute p1 = 1 + p2: 3(1+p2) + 2p2 = 1 => 3 + 3p2 + 2p2 = 1 => 5p2 = -2.
    No.
    What about {A0, A2, A3}?
    E(2) = 4 - 2p0
    E(4) = 6 - 6p0 - 2p2
    E(5) = 5 - 5p0 - 3p2
    Already checked, led to p0 = 1.

    Let me reconsider. Is there *any* mixed Nash equilibrium?
    I should check all combinations of strategies.
    I've checked:
    - {A0, A1, A2, A3} -> None
    - {A0, A1, A2} -> None
    - {A1, A2, A3} -> None
    - {A0, A1, A3} -> E(2)=4-2p0, E(3)=5-5p0-2p1, E(5)=5-5p0-4p1. (p3 = 1-p0-p1)
    Setting E(3)=E(5): 5-5p0-2p1 = 5-5p0-4p1 => 2p1 = 0 => p1 = 0.
    Back to {A0, A3}, which I haven't checked.
    E(2) = 4 - 2p0
    E(5) = 5 - 5p0
    Set E(2)=E(5): 4 - 2p0 = 5 - 5p0 => 3p0 = 1 => p0 = 1/3, p3 = 2/3.
    If p0 = 1/3 and p3 = 2/3, let's check E(2) and E(5):
    E(2) = 4 - 2(1/3) = 12/3 - 2/3 = 10/3 = 3.33
    E(5) = 5 - 5(1/3) = 15/3 - 5/3 = 10/3 = 3.33
    Now check E(3) and E(4) for these probabilities:
    E(3) = 0p0 + 3(0) + 5p3 = 5(2/3) = 10/3 = 3.33
    E(4) = 0p0 + 1(0) + 6p3 = 6(2/3) = 12/3 = 4.0
    Wait! E(4) is 4.0, which is higher than 3.33. So {A0, A3} is not an equilibrium.

    Is there any other combination?
    What about {A0, A1}?
    4 - 2p0 = 3 - 3p0 => p0 = -1. No.
    What about {A2, A3}?
    E(4) = 4p2 + 6p3 = 4p2 + 6(1-p2) = 6 - 2p2
    E(5) = 2p2 + 5p3 = 2p2 + 5(1-p2) = 5 - 3p2
    Set E(4)=E(5): 6-2p2 = 5-3p2 => p2 = -1. No.
    What about {A1, A3}?
    E(3) = 3p1 + 5p3 = 3p1 + 5(1-p1) = 5 - 2p1
    E(5) = 1p1 + 5p3 = 1p1 + 5(1-p1) = 5 - 4p1
    Set E(3)=E(5): 5-2p1 = 5-4p1 => p1 = 0. No.
    What about {A1, A2}?
    E(3) = 3p1 + 5p2 = 3p1 + 5(1-p1) = 5 - 2p1
    E(4) = 1p1 + 4p2 = 1p1 + 4(1-p1) = 4 - 3p1
    Set E(3)=E(4): 5-2p1 = 4-3p1 => p1 = -1. No.

    Conclusion: The only Nash Equilibrium is p0 = 1. (A0 = 100%)

    Wait! Let me double-check the calculation:
    Set p1=1/3, p2=2/3, p0=0, p3=0.
    E(3) = 3(1/3) + 5(2/3) = 1 + 10/3 = 13/3 = 4.33
    E(4) = 1(1/3) + 4(2/3) = 1/3 + 8/3 = 9/3 = 3.0
    E(2) = 2p0 + 4p1 + 4p2 + 4p3 = 4(1/3) + 4(2/3) = 4.0
    Not an equilibrium because E(3) > E(2) and E(3) > E(4).

    Wait, let's re-check the {A2, A3} case if I made a mistake.
    E(4) = 4p2 + 6p3
    E(5) = 2p2 + 5p3
    If p2 + p3 = 1, then E(4) is always greater than E(5) because 4>2 and 6>5. (Dominance!)
    So {A2, A3} could never be an equilibrium.

    Wait, what about {A0, A1, A3}?
    E(2) = 2p0 + 4p1 + 4p3 = 4 - 2p0
    E(3) = 0p0 + 3p1 + 5p3 = 3p1 + 5(1-p0-p1) = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1
    E(5) = 0p0 + 1p1 + 5p3 = p1 + 5(1-p0-p1) = 5 - 5p0 - 4p1
    To have E(3)=E(5), we need p1=0.
    If p1=0, we're back to {A0, A3}, which we already checked.

    What about {A0, A2}?
    E(2) = 2p0 + 4p2 = 2p0 + 4(1-p0) = 4 - 2p0
    E(4) = 0p0 + 4p2 = 4(1-p0) = 4 - 4p0
    To have E(2)=E(4), we need 4-2p0 = 4-4p0 => p0=0.
    If p0=0, p2=1. We're back to the pure strategy Action 4. But we know A3 is the best response to A4. Let's check:
    If he plays A4 (p2=1), my payoffs are:
    E(2) = 4
    E(3) = 5
    E(4) = 4
    E(5) = 2
    So my best response to A4 is A3.

    Wait... let's check {A0, A1, A2}.
    E(2) = 4 - 2p0
    E(3) = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1
    E(4) = 4 - 4p0 - 3p1
    For E(2)=E(3): 4-2p0 = 5-5p0-2p1 => 3p0 + 2p1 = 1
    For E(3)=E(4): 5-5p0-2p1 = 4-4p0-3p1 => p1 - p0 = -1 => p0 - p1 = 1
    If p0 - p1 = 1, then since p0 <= 1 and p1 >= 0, p0 must be 1 and p1 must be 0.
    So once again, we are at the pure strategy A0.

    So, after checking all combinations, only (A0, A0) is a Nash equilibrium.
    Is it possible I have a typo in the payoff matrix? Let me re-read the rules one more time.
    X=Y: you get X
    X<Y: you get X+2
    X>Y: you get Y-2

    Let's check the values again:
    2, 2: 2
    2, 3: 2<3, I get 2+2=4
    2, 4: 2<4, I get 2+2=4
    2, 5: 2<5, I get 2+2=4

    3, 2: 3>2, I get 2-2=0
    3, 3: 3
    3, 4: 3<4, I get 3+2=5
    3, 5: 3<5, I get 3+2=5

    4, 2: 4>2, I get 2-2=0
    4, 3: 4>3, I get 3-2=1
    4, 4: 4
    4, 5: 4<5, I get 4+2=6

    5, 2: 5>2, I get 2-2=0
    5, 3: 5>3, I get 3-2=1
    5, 4: 5>4, I get 4-2=2
    5, 5: 5

    Yes, the matrix is absolutely correct.
    Wait, let's just double check the "other player" part.
    "If X < Y: you get X + 2.0, the other player gets X - 2.0."
    If I (X) am 2 and he (Y) is 3, then X < Y.
    I get X + 2 = 4.
    He gets X - 2 = 2 - 2 = 0.
    Wait! If he chooses 3 and I choose 2, I get 4 and he gets 0.
    If we both chose 2, we'd both get 2.
    If we both chose 3, we'd both get 3.
    This is what I used to find the Nash Equilibrium. (2,2) is the only NE.

    Is there any reason to play something else? In some games, people might choose A3(5) to be cooperative.
    But this is not a cooperative game. It's a "maximize your total points" game.
    If I play A0(2), I am guaranteed 2 points, no matter what they do.
    If they play A0(2), I get 2.
    If they play A1(3), I get 4.
    If they play A2(4), I get 4.
    If they play A3(5), I get 4.

    Wait, what if they also play A0(2)? Then we both get 2.
    Could we get more?
    If we both knew the other would play A1(3), we'd get 3.
    But if I suspect they might play A1(3), my best response is A0(2) to get 4!
    There is no way to get more than 2 points unless the other player is irrational and chooses a higher number, in which case I should choose A0 (to get 4) OR another number just below theirs (to get 5 or 6).
    But A0(2) is the *safest* way to get 4 points if they play anything else.
    Wait, let's check that.
    If they play A2(4), my options are:
    A0(2): 4 points
    A1(3): 5 points
    A2(4): 4 points
    A3(5): 2 points
    So if they play A2(4), I should play A1(3).
    But if they play A1(3), I should play A0(2) (4 points vs 3, 1, 1).
    So it always comes back to A0(2).

    Let me think about the other player. Will they play A0?
    In many situations, especially in an AI context, the common choice for a Traveler's Dilemma is to choose the Nash Equilibrium or something close to it.
    Given that A0 is the only Nash Equilibrium and also the minimax choice, it's a very strong candidate.

    What about the possibility of playing a mix?
    The instruction says to "Choose a probability distribution over ALL actions each round." and "maximize your total points".
    If I play 100% A0, and the other person also plays 100% A0, we each get 2.
    If I play something like 97% A0, 1% A1, 1% A2, 1% A3, I am just slightly lowering my expectation if they play A0.
    Is there any benefit to putting some weight on other actions?
    Not really, in a single-shot game. If it was a multi-round game, maybe I could try to signal cooperation, but even then, signaling in a simultaneous game with no communication is hard.
    Wait, the format requirement says "Values must be percentage points given in integers. All values must sum to exactly 100."
    So I can't even play 99.5%.

    How about 100 on A0?
    {"A0": 100, "A1": 0, "A2": 0, "A3": 0}

    Wait, I'll just do a quick final check.
    If the opponent is an AI, it might also reach the same conclusion.
    If both AIs play A0: 100, they both get 2.
    Is there any other strategy that could do better against some range of opponents?
    If the opponent plays randomly, A0 gets 3.5.
    If the opponent plays A0, A0 gets 2.
    If the opponent plays A1, A0 gets 4.
    If the opponent plays A2, A0 gets 4.
    If the opponent plays A3, A0 gets 4.

    Wait, what if the opponent plays like a human and picks 5?
    If they pick 5:
    A0: 4
    A1: 5
    A2: 6
    A3: 5
    So A2 would be the best.
    But if they pick 5 *some* of the time, and pick 2 *some* of the time, let's see:
    If they pick 2 with probability p, and 5 with probability (1-p):
    E(0) = 2p + 4(1-p) = 4 - 2p
    E(1) = 0p + 5(1-p) = 5 - 5p
    E(2) = 0p + 6(1-p) = 6 - 6p
    E(3) = 0p + 5(1-p) = 5 - 5p
    Compare E(0) and E(2):
    4-2p vs 6-6p
    4p vs 2
    p vs 0.5
    So if p > 0.5 (they pick 2 more than 50% of the time), A0 is better than A2.
    If p < 0.5 (they pick 5 more than 50% of the time), A2 is better than A0.

    Is it likely that the other player will pick 2 more than 50% of the time?
    Given that Row 0 (X=2) is the only NE and the minimax strategy, I'd say yes.
    Actually, let's consider a mix. If I'm wrong and they play more 5s, 4s, or 3s, putting a little bit of probability on A1 or A2 could be beneficial. But if they're playing 2, then any probability on A1, A2, or A3 will give me 0 instead of 2.

    Let me consider the possibility that I should play a little more than just A0.
    But wait, the game is "maximize your *total points*". If this is a single round, the most logical thing to do in a game like this is to play the NE.

    One final look: is there any other possible interpretation of the rules?
    - If X = Y: you get X
    - If X < Y: you get X + 2
    - If X > Y: you get Y - 2

    Let me re-calculate again:
    X=2, Y=3: X<Y, 2+2=4.
    X=2, Y=4: X<Y, 2+2=4.
    X=2, Y=5: X<Y, 2+2=4.
    X=3, Y=2: X>Y, 2-2=0.
    X=3, Y=4: X<Y, 3+2=5.
    X=3, Y=5: X<Y, 3+2=5.
    X=4, Y=2: X>Y, 2-2=0.
    X=4, Y=3: X>Y, 3-2=1.
    X=4, Y=5: X<Y, 4+2=6.
    X=5, Y=2: X>Y, 2-2=0.
    X=5, Y=3: X>Y, 3-2=1.
    X=5, Y=4: X>Y, 4-2=2.

    Everything seems solid.
    Nash equilibrium at A0 = 100%.
    Minimax strategy at A0 = 100%.
    The result of iterated elimination of weakly dominated strategies is A0 = 100%.
    The highest average payoff against a uniform random opponent is A0 = 100%.
    The only reason to play anything else is if I'm very sure the other player will play 4 or 5.
    But if they are rational, they'll play 2. Even if they're irrational, they'd have to be *very* irrational for E(3) or E(4) to be better than E(2).
    Let's check E(2) vs E(3) one more time.
    E(2) = 4 - 2p0
    E(3) = 0p0 + 3p1 + 5p2 + 5p3 = 3p1 + 5(1-p0-p1) = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1
    For E(3) > E(2):
    5 - 5p0 - 2p1 > 4 - 2p0
    1 - 3p0 - 2p1 > 0
    This means the other player must play 2 less than 1/3 of the time. (For example, if p0=0.1 and p1=0.1, 1 - 0.3 - 0.2 = 0.5 > 0.)
    Is it plausible that the other player avoids the Nash equilibrium?
    In many experimental studies, it is! But in this version of the dilemma, the stakes are so small (2, 3, 4, 5) that it's much more likely they'll land on 2.

    Let me consider the option of putting 100% on A0. That's the most mathematically sound answer.
    {"A0": 100, "A1": 0, "A2": 0, "A3": 0}
    There's no reason to think the other player would choose a different strategy unless I have information about them.
    Wait, could the other AI be thinking "the other player will choose 2, so I'll choose 2 too"?
    If they think "the other player will choose 2", their best response is 2.
    If they think "the other player will choose 3", their best response is 2.
    If they think "the other player will choose 4", their best response is 3.
    If they think "the other player will choose 5", their best response is 4.

    Wait, let's look at the matrix one more time.
    If I choose X=3 and they choose Y=4, I get 5.
    If I choose X=2 and they choose Y=4, I get 4.
    But choosing X=3 is very risky. If they choose Y=2, I get 0.
    Choosing X=2 is much safer. Even if they choose Y=4, I still get 4.
    So the difference between A0 and A1 is:
    - If they play 2: A0 gives 2, A1 gives 0. (A0 is +2)
    - If they play 3: A0 gives 4, A1 gives 3. (A0 is +1)
    - If they play 4: A0 gives 4, A1 gives 5. (A1 is +1)
    - If they play 5: A0 gives 4, A1 gives 5. (A1 is +1)

    So A0 is better if they play 2 or 3.
    A1 is better if they play 4 or 5.
